Subsequently, a part of exports from the Czech Republic was compared with imports to the Slovak Republic in 2002 and vice versa for selected commodities according to the customs tariff:
- 2207 Undenatured ethyl alcohol of an alcoholic strength by volume of 80% vol or higher; ethyl alcohol and other spirits, denatured, of any strength,
- 2208 Undenatured ethyl alcohol of an alcoholic strength by volume of less than 80% vol; spirits, liqueurs and other spirituous beverages,
- 2401 Unmanufactured tobacco; tobacco waste,
- 2402 Cigars, cheroots, cigarillos and cigarettes, of tobacco or of tobacco substitutes,
- 2403 Other manufactured tobacco and tobacco substitutes; homogenised or reconstituted tobacco; tobacco extracts and essences;
- 8525 20 91 Transmission apparatus containing reception equipment: For cellular network (mobile phones),
- 8525 20 99 Transmission apparatus incorporating reception apparatus:
- 8529 90 40 Parts suitable for use solely or principally with the apparatus of headings 8525 to 8528.
Other: Parts of apparatus falling in subheadings 8525 10 50, 8525 20 91, 8525 20 99, 8525 40 11 and 8527 90 92.
JCD items from the customs databases of the Czech Republic and Slovakia were mutually assigned based on the agreement of the individual paragraphs of the JCD (e.g. invoiced price, quantity, weight, registration number of the vehicle transporting the load, date of crossing the national border, tariff item, etc.).
The results of the comparison are shown in the following tables and graphs.
The cross-referencing of individual imports and exports was hampered, inter alia, by the fact that commercial transactions were not declared in the same way on both sides.
For example, exports from the Czech Republic declared on one export JCD were declared on the territory of the Slovak Republic on multiple import JCDs, or the export JCD had a different number of items from the import JCD.
Other factors that affected the success of the referencing were the different composition of data in the customs databases of the Czech Republic and Slovakia, as well as the time shift between the registration of exports in the customs database of one country and the registration of corresponding imports in the customs database of the other country.
The results of the comparison were used in the selection of control samples at audited financial and tax offices.
Priority was given to certain tax entities that carried out imports or exports between the Czech Republic and Slovakia that could not be identified in both databases.
In addition, some tax entities were selected for the verification of business transactions reported in VAT returns, for which data on imports or exports between the Czech Republic and Slovakia were found in both databases.
4.3. Pilot check at the border customs crossing Starý Hrozenkov-Drietoma
The border customs crossing point for pilot control was selected on the basis of a joint analysis of unassigned JCD items in the customs databases of the Czech Republic and Slovakia.
On the basis of a bilateral agreement between the customs authorities of the Czech Republic and Slovakia, the use of control sheets was introduced at the border customs crossing points between the Czech Republic and Slovakia from 1.6.1998.
The aim of this measure was to eliminate customs and tax evasion as far as possible, to ensure an overview of the movement of commercial transport vehicles in the customs area and to create the conditions for comparing customs registers with each other.
The joint pilot inspection was carried out between 8.3.2004 and 26.3.2004 and aimed at examining this customs control system.
Procedure for the use of checklists at the Old Hrozenkov-Drietoma border crossing point:
1) Weighing the vehicle, issuing the control sheet and handing it over to the driver.
2) Parking of the vehicle in the designated sector in the parking lot.
Customs clearance in the check-in hall of the Police of the Czech Republic, Police of the Slovak Republic, Customs Administration of the Czech Republic, Customs Administration of the Slovak Republic.
The check-in was confirmed by individual files stamping on the inspection sheet.
3) Bringing the vehicle into the physical inspection area.
Presentation of the inspection sheet, JCD, or other documents to the customs official - verification of the completion of all prescribed acts and documents.
Performance of a physical check by the appropriate ingredients.
Release of a vehicle from the customs area.
Note: When importing goods into the Czech Republic, the weighing of the vehicle was carried out directly by employees of the Customs Administration of the Czech Republic.
When the goods were imported into the Slovak Republic, the weighing of the vehicles was carried out by the staff of the authorised legal entity.
The vehicles were registered in the computerised register after weighing.

From the number of 453 JCD items, the SAO found 256 cases of imports into the Slovak Republic according to data from the customs database of the Czech Republic and Slovakia. The remaining 44 cases were identified in the register by the employees of the border customs crossing Drietoma and the export of goods from the Slovak Republic to the Czech Republic was confirmed.
The SAO and SAO audit did not find any deficiencies in the registration of selected cases of imports and exports of goods in 2002 at the border customs crossing Starý Hrozenkov-Drietoma.
4.4. Selected audit findings when verifying commercial transactions
Within the framework of international cooperation, the realization of imports and exports between the Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic was verified from documents of customs administrations and financial or tax offices in the territory of the other state.
For selected tax entities, the following were verified:
- whether the submitted JCDs were registered in the customs databases of the Czech Republic and Slovakia,
- the fulfilment of the legal conditions for the application of the deduction of VAT on importation, the refund of excess VAT and the application of the VAT exemption on exportation.
The verification of commercial transactions revealed:
1. The SAO of Slovakia requested verification of the export of alcohol in the statistical value of 56 989 Sk.
The SAO identified the corresponding imports in the Czech customs database with a statistical value of CZK 42,624. r
2. A company engaged in the import and export of photographic equipment and accessories claimed an excess VAT deduction of CZK 3,740,664 in its VAT return for the month of December 2002.
In order to check reported exports from the Czech Republic, the SAO selected 3 JCD (16 items) with a statistical value of CZK 1,274,822.
The SAO of Slovakia identified 3 corresponding import JCDs (17 items) with a statistical value of 1,724,321 CZK.
VAT was paid to the competent customs office in the Slovak Republic.
3. In 2002, a company whose main business was the trade in optical equipment claimed a total excess VAT deduction of CZK 4 1 36 000 higher than the tax liability.
The SAO selected an export JCD (32 items) with a statistical value of CZK 7,154,124 to verify the reported business activity.
The SAO of Slovakia found that this JCD corresponds to the import JCD (12 items) with a statistical value of 9 653 827 Sk.
VAT was paid to the competent customs office in the Slovak Republic.
4. The company involved in, inter alia, the purchase of goods for resale and sale did not report any exports of goods in the VAT returns filed in 2002 and 2003.
According to the customs database of the Czech Republic, the statistical value of goods exports in 2002 totalled CZK 3,439,888.
Based on these facts, the SAO selected 5 export JCDs (5 items) for verification in the total statistical value of CZK 669,839.
The SAO of Slovakia identified 5 corresponding import JCDs (5 items) with a statistical value of 1 012 656 Sk.
The audit of the SAO of the Slovak Republic at the relevant tax office showed that the declared imports were made and VAT was paid.
5. The company, whose business was, among other things, the purchase of goods for resale and sale, production, installation and repair of electrical machinery and appliances, reported in 2002 in VAT returns the export of goods worth CZK 12,966,706.
According to the Czech customs database, the total value of exports in 2002 was CZK 14,474,381.
The SAO submitted 17 export JCDs (59 items) with a total statistical value of CZK 2,975,122.
The SAO of Slovakia identified 13 JCD (50 items) in the customs database SR with a statistical value of 3 621 370 Sk.
The Slovak customs database failed to identify the corresponding import JCD to 4 export JCDs (9 items) with a total statistical value of CZK 399,776.
The relevant tax office in SR verified these imports with the taxpayer.
The tax entity listed on the JCD as an importer to the Slovak Republic stated in the minutes of the local investigation that it did not carry out the imports declared on the Czech export JCD.
The SAO of the Slovak Republic therefore asked the local tax office to carry out a tax audit of VAT and corporate income tax for the tax years 2002 and 2003.
6. According to the customs database of the Czech Republic, a company engaged in the import and sale of cigarettes exported tobacco stickers in 2002 with a statistical value of CZK 7,338,199 and in 2003 with a statistical value of CZK 12,228,545.
The company did not report any exports in its VAT returns in 2002 and 2003.
The SAO selected 5 export JCDs (5 items) for verification in the total statistical value of CZK 2,471,598.
For 4 export JCDs (4 items) in the total statistical value of 2 246 860 CZK, the SAO SR identified 7 JCDs (7 items) in the customs database SR in the statistical value of 2 919 514 CZK.
The export JCD (1 item) in the statistical value of CZK 224,738 failed to assign the corresponding import in the customs database of SR.
7. The software company did not report exports of goods in its 2002 and 2003 VAT returns.
According to the Czech customs database, the statistical value of goods exports in 2002 and 2003 totalled CZK 1,630,198.
The SAO selected 9 JCD (9 items) for verification in SR, totalling CZK 755,588.
In the customs database SR SAO SR identified 8 JCD (8 items) in statistical value 915 601 Sk.
The export JCD (1 item) with a statistical value of CZK 68,286 could not be assigned to the corresponding import in the SR customs database.
In these cases, it was not possible to identify the corresponding imports to 6 JCD in the customs database with a statistical value of CZK 692,800.
Of which:
- to 2 export JCDs from the customs database of the Czech Republic with a statistical value of 293 024 CZK, the corresponding import JCD could not be identified in the customs database of the Slovak Republic (see points 6 and 7).
Due to a certain error rate in entering data in the customs database, it was not possible to identify certain registered imports, e.g. in cases where the importer's name was missing or there was an error in the tax identification number (TIN).
Therefore, it cannot be concluded with certainty that these exports did not take place.
- to 4 export JCDs from the customs database of the Czech Republic, imports corresponding to exports from the Czech Republic to the Slovak Republic in the statistical value of CZK 399,776 were not proven (see point 5).
In such cases, there are reasonable grounds for suspecting that goods have been fictitiously exported for the purpose of wrongfully claiming a VAT deduction.
5. Control of imports and exports of goods and services within the scope of control of selected financial and tax authorities
5.1. Control sample
In order to verify the fulfilment of the obligations of tax entities in the import and export of goods and services and to evaluate the level of cooperation between the tax and customs authorities, the files of tax entities were selected at the audited financial and tax offices according to the following criteria:
1. tax subjects were selected from the customs databases of the Czech Republic and Slovakia: (a) with the highest volume of imports, (b) with the highest amount of VAT charged, (c) with the highest volume of exports,
2. tax entities were selected from the register of the tax or tax authorities according to the amount of: (a) VAT deductions applied on importation, (b) excess VAT deductions applied, (c) VAT refund entitlement, (d) VAT exemption applied on exportation,
3. tax entities that have been subject to a tax audit.
The sampling was carried out irrespective of the country of destination, with a focus on risky commodities.
5.2. Inspection findings in the Czech Republic
An overview of imports into the Czech Republic and exports from the Czech Republic for risky commodities in 2002 and 2003 is given in the following table:
5.2.1. Inspection of selected sample at financial offices
The SAO focused on:
- the procedure of the tax authorities in verifying the correctness of reported VAT,
- cooperation between the tax authorities and the customs administration,
- comparison of data from the customs database with the JCD database at the tax offices, focusing on the comparison of the declared value, the application of the correct VAT rate following the customs classification of goods or services, or comparison of the data on the JCD and the accompanying invoices with the data in the customs database,
- comparison of data from VAT returns on transactions carried out and received from abroad with data in the JCD database at tax offices or in the customs database;
- how to verify the right to VAT exemption when exporting goods or providing services abroad,
- a comparison of the volume of exports of goods or services declared in VAT returns with data from the customs database.
The JCD database at the tax offices contained only records of those imports and exports for which taxable persons were obliged to include the import or export in the tax return.
The audited tax authorities examined the files of tax subjects who had reported imports totalling CZK 60,396,379,000 in the 2002 and 2003 VAT returns and corresponding VAT totalling CZK 12,644,052,000.
In addition, the files of tax entities reporting exports in the tax returns for 2002 and 2003 totalling CZK 47,323,111,000 were checked.
According to the Customs Database of the Czech Republic, in 2002 and 2003, the tax subjects whose imports and exports between the Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic were examined carried out imports into the Czech Republic from the Slovak Republic with a total VAT base of 3 759 352 thousand CZK, VAT of 767 607 thousand CZK and exports from the Czech Republic to the Slovak Republic with a total statistical value of 1 989 090 thousand CZK.
The identified differences between the records in the JCD database at the tax offices and in the customs database of the Czech Republic were examined as follows:
- JCD items that were missing from the customs database of the Czech Republic were requested directly at the General Directorate of Customs of the Czech Republic,
- JCD items that were missing from the Tax Offices database were checked for cancellations,
- for JCD items for which different values were found between the customs database of the Czech Republic and the database of financial offices, the respective JCD was requested from the General Directorate of Customs of the Czech Republic in written form including the attached invoices.
The audit revealed that the JCD database at the tax offices did not contain, for example, records of import JCDs with a total value of VAT of CZK 3,622,000 and records of export JCDs with a total statistical value of CZK 9,359,000.
5.2.1.1. Differences found
A comparison of the JCD database at the tax offices and the cumulative tax liabilities for 2002 and 2003 in the VAT returns for a selected sample of tax entities revealed the following differences:
The differences in values were mainly due to:
- The time difference between the realisation of imports and the exercise of the right to deduct VAT charged on the importation of goods.
The taxpayer could exercise the right of deduction in respect of the importation of goods within three years of the end of the tax year in which the goods were placed under the relevant scheme or, in the case of the temporary importation scheme, within three years of the end of the tax year in which that scheme ceased.
- The time difference between the realisation of the export and the moment when the taxpayer received the JCD.
The taxpayer was required to enter the details of the exports carried out exempt from the obligation to apply output tax in the VAT return at the earliest for the tax period in which the goods left the territory of the country if he also had the tax document which was the JCD.
- Failure of taxable persons to comply with the obligations to declare imports and exports in VAT returns.
In some cases, tax subjects did not include correct data on imports and exports in their VAT returns.
The fact that tax subjects were not required to attach JCD lists to their VAT returns made it more difficult for the tax authorities to check the right to deduct or exempt VAT.
5.2.2. Synergies between financial and customs authorities
During the audited period, refunds or remissions of import or export duties and VAT were made on the basis of decisions taken by the customs authorities.
Tax subjects were required by law to reduce the previously exercised right to deduct VAT by the amount of tax refunded by the customs office in the tax return for the period in which the tax was refunded.
No legislation provided for the sending of information on changes in the amount of customs duties and VAT from customs offices to tax offices.
Some audited tax offices received this information, but irregularly or with significant delays and only from some customs offices.
One audited IRS did not receive this information at all.
If the tax office did not receive information about changes in the amount of customs duties and VAT directly from the customs office, it did not have the data needed to check the fulfilment of the legal obligation of the tax subjects.
In 2002, the customs authorities refunded VAT in the amount of more than CZK 319 million and in 2003 in the amount of more than CZK 398 million.
The results of the audit showed persistent shortcomings in the activities of the tax authorities, some systemic errors in the cooperation of the tax authorities with the customs authorities and the non-fulfilment of some obligations of the tax payers submitting VAT and VAT returns.
In particular, weaknesses were found in the administration of VAT and VAT in the following areas:
a) in case of cancellation of VAT registrations, the fulfilment of the obligation of the payers to pay VAT on the commercial property registered or which should have been registered on the date of cancellation of the registration, with which the payer applied a tax deduction, was verified only rarely,
(b) the tax authorities have not consistently used the possibility to carry out tax inspections or local investigations.
This has in some cases led to a lack of verification of the validity of the right to deduct VAT.
(c) the official records and minutes of the oral hearing do not contain all the necessary data to substantiate the legitimacy of the excess VAT deductions applied;
(d) the tax authorities did not sufficiently verify the imports and exports declared in the VAT returns.
5.3. Control findings in SR
5.3.1. Inspection of selected sample at tax offices
The SAO scrutinized or audited the selected sample for tax years 2002 and 2003.
After analysing the submitted tax returns and requests for tax refunds from selected tax entities of the SAO SR, the Tax Office requested to secure the relevant JCDs documenting the import and export, invoices and proof of payment of the tax.
Subsequently, the SAO scrutinized whether the submitted JCDs were registered in the customs database of imports and exports and whether the decisive facts and legal conditions were fulfilled in the application of the deduction of input tax for imports, in the refund of excess VAT deduction, in the application of the exemption from export tax and in the refund of the SPD.
The SAO of the Slovak Republic audited selected cases of imports and exports of goods between the Slovak Republic and the Czech Republic according to the number of JCD items and the sum of statistical values listed in the following table.
In total, imports in the statistical value of 856.2 million SKK were checked, which represents 5.11% of the total value of imports of selected commodities from the Czech Republic into the SROV.
Exports of selected commodities were checked in the total statistical value of SKK 176 million, which represents 6.87% of the total value of exports of selected commodities from the Slovak Republic to the Czech Republic.
In addition, 618 cases of imports of other goods and services (mainly white goods, packaging technology and software) from EU countries to Slovakia were checked for a total statistical value of SKK 532.8 million.
5.3.2. Synergies between tax and customs authorities
The SAO scrutinized the tax offices and found discrepancies between the data recorded in the customs database of the Slovak Republic and the data reported in specific VAT returns, e.g.:
– 2 tax subjects (natural persons) declared imports of goods with a total value of SKK 1 589 261 in their tax returns, on the basis of which they claimed a refund of excess VAT.
During local investigations carried out at the initiative of the SAO, tax subjects were not able to prove to the tax office the validity of the application of the input tax by the relevant documents on the completion of imports and payment of VAT.
- the tax entity (s. r. o.) in the tax return for the tax period January 2003 declared the received taxable transaction in the value of SKK 77 167 576, the export of goods in the value of SKK 18 006 041 and applied an excess VAT deduction in the amount of SKK 17 157 101.
It is apparent from the VAT returns filed for 2003 that the difference between the volume of purchases and exports of SKK 59 161 535, which arose in January 2003, was not made up during the whole of 2003.
In the above cases, the SAO asked the relevant tax authorities to carry out a tax audit of the applied excess VAT deductions.
- in another case, the tax entity (s. r. o.) for the tax period January to June 2002 indicated in the VAT returns the export of goods with a total value of SKK 87 199.
A verification in the customs database of the Slovak Republic revealed that the taxpayer had exported a statistical value of SKK 296 747 during that period.
The Tax Office carried out several on-the-spot inspections at the company's registered office and found that the company's managing director (a UK citizen) had travelled and the company had left the leased premises without terminating the lease in August 2002.
At the time of the SAO audit, the tax administrator filed a report with the law enforcement authorities on suspicion of tax evasion.
The SAO of Slovakia, based on the results of the audit at selected tax offices, states that the biggest problems in the administration and control of VAT and VAT were mainly in the following areas:
- failure to make use of the possibility to carry out VAT tax audits on problematic tax subjects, in particular in cases of application of a high value of excess VAT deductions;
- the tax inspection reports did not contain an inventory of the documents checked
- JCD on import and export of goods,
- insufficient screening of declared imports and exports of goods in tax returns;
- a lengthy procedure for tax authorities to take decisions, in particular in the context of the assessment procedure, with the consequence that the right to assess penalties is extinguished;
- non-compliance with legal conditions when granting tax deferrals,
- failure to compensate VAT arrears within the deadline for the refund of excess VAT,
- insufficient use of all forms of security and recovery of tax arrears.
On the basis of the audited shortcomings, the SAO of the Slovak Republic requested the relevant tax authorities to carry out a tax audit of VAT with a focus on the validity of applied excess VAT deductions for 14 tax entities.
Measures were taken at all audited tax offices to remedy the deficiencies detected by the audit, the performance of which will be monitored and audited by the SAO SR.
6. Conclusion
International cooperation has made it possible to examine trade transactions between trading partners from both countries and has highlighted shortcomings in the registration of imports and exports.
The inspections also revealed persistent deficiencies in the fulfilment of the obligations of VAT and VAT payers, in the activities of the financial and tax authorities, in the system of cooperation and exchange of information between tax and customs administrations, which ultimately reduced the effectiveness and efficiency of tax inspections.
The findings also showed that the tax authorities in the Czech Republic have at their disposal within the tax information system basic data on imports and exports from the customs database of the Czech Republic. In SR, there is no similar system for transmitting information on imports and exports of goods between customs and tax administrations.
The procedure for verifying the performance of the VAT and VAT administration was not the same due to the different legislation and conditions in the two countries.
The SAO verified the necessary data from information obtained at the Czech Customs Administration and from documents available at audited financial offices. The SAO verified the execution of commercial transactions from documents of tax entities requested through tax authorities.
If irregularities were found, he asked the tax authorities to carry out tax inspections.
Parallel controls carried out in the area of VAT and SPD have assessed the state of administration of these taxes in the period prior to accession to the European Union and pointed to the need for further cooperation in this area.
Mutual cooperation will be further enhanced in the monitoring of the activities of the financial and tax authorities in connection with the adoption of the new VAT Act.
One of the new obligations relating to the delivery of goods to other Member States of the European Union is the submission of a so-called recapitulative statement.
This declaration must be made by all VAT payers who have made a supply of goods to a person registered for VAT in another Member State.
Tax administrators are obliged to enter the data from the recapitulative statements into the VIES (VAT Information Exchange System), to which tax administrators from the Member States of the European Union have access.
The tax or tax authorities then have the possibility to compare these data with the data contained in the submitted VAT returns.
Furthermore, this system is used to verify the validity of the VAT registration number of persons registered for VAT, to verify the validity of the VAT exemption applied to the supply of goods to another Member State and to check the proper taxation of goods.
The implementation of parallel audits showed the possibilities of cooperation between the SAO and the SAO of Slovakia.
The results of these control actions will be confronted with the outputs of the upcoming control actions, which will focus, among other things, on the examination of the VIES system, which is intended to prevent tax evasion in so-called "intra-community deliveries".
Given the importance of VAT and SPD for budgetary revenue, control activities need to focus primarily on monitoring and identifying risk areas in order to reduce the scope for tax evasion of indirect taxes.
Communiqué
This report was drawn up in the Czech, English and Slovak languages and signed by the Vice-President of the Supreme Audit Office and the President of the Supreme Audit Office of the Slovak Republic.
Report on the results of the check in asylum facilities in the Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic
State property and funds intended for securing the activities of the Administration of Refugee Facilities of the Ministry of the Interior
Audit action No. 04/23 "State property and funds intended for securing the activities of the Refugee Facilities Administration of the Ministry of the Interior"
Control action No 15/2004 "Control of hospodárenia with the property of the state and financial means intended to provide asylum and temporary refuge cudzincom in SR"
1. Introduction
Population migration is one of the global phenomena that have become an inseparable part of the current development phase of the Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic.
It is mainly caused by the flight of citizens from their country of origin or last permanent residence from persecution, in particular for racial, religious, national, political reasons, as well as from the consequences of military conflicts and ethnic cleansing.
Their aim is to obtain asylum and integration into society, especially in one of the advanced democratic countries of Europe.
A large group also consists of foreigners whose migration is caused by unfavourable economic and living conditions.
Migration is not a new phenomenon.
The adoption of the 1951 Geneva Agreement and the 1967 New York Protocol on the Status of Refugees, by which the governments of the signatory countries undertook to apply them in their foreign policy orientations, already provided concrete principles for refugee assistance.
The Czech and Slovak Federative Republic ratified the New York Protocol in 1991 and the Geneva Agreement in 1992.
After the split in 1993, both the Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic declared their position on the refugee issue by continuing to comply with the adopted international documents and confirmed their binding force within the framework of national legislation by a legal act.
Both republics have enshrined the granting of asylum in their constitutional laws.
Parallel checks carried out by the Supreme Audit Office, the Czech Republic and the Supreme Audit Office of the Slovak Republic focused on the asylum process and related issues, the results of which are contained in a joint final report.
The cooperation between the SAIs of both countries was implemented on the basis of the "Contract for Cooperation in Control Activities" (hereinafter referred to as "the Contract") concluded in April 2004.
The two Parties have undertaken in the Treaty to provide each other with information on the legislation governing the asylum procedure in the other Contracting Party, selected statistics on the number of asylum seekers and the results of the asylum process, as well as information on asylum facilities.
The aim of the parallel control action was to identify the same problem areas and to highlight the consequences that this entails for population migration.
Cooperation consisted of the unification of the programme of control actions, the exchange of information to the extent provided for in the Treaty and the preparation of a joint final report on the outcome of the controls.
During the first joint meeting, which took place from 5.4. to 7.4.2004, the same topics of audited issues and control procedures were established in the preparation of audits.
At the second meeting, which took place from 28.6 to 30.6.2004, the common points for the programme of the audit action were specified.
During the third joint meeting, which took place during the audit operation from 8.11 to 10.11.2004, an exchange of experience from the on-going inspections took place.
The forms of the outputs from the checks have been specified and the structure and content of the Joint Final Report, which was drawn up between 14.2 and 17.2.2005 at the last joint meeting, have been preliminarily established.
The checks were carried out in parallel between September 2004 and January 2005.
In the Czech Republic, the audit was carried out by inspectors of the Defence and Security Department and selected territorial departments.
The audited bodies were the Ministry of the Interior (hereinafter referred to as "MV") and the Refugee Facilities Administration of the Ministry of the Interior (hereinafter referred to as "SUZ").
In the Slovak Republic carried out the inspection of the section of defence, vnútra and štátnych úradov.
The audited entity was the Migration Office of the Ministry vnútra SR (MÚ).
The audited period covered the years 2001, 2002 and 2003 (the audited period).
In some cases, statistical data covering a longer period of time are also used in the joint report.
2. Recapitulation of control results
2.1. Evaluation of control activities
- the asylum process, - the asylum facilities, - the management of state funds and assets, - the internal control system.
The mutual exchange of gained experience between the Supreme Audit Office of the Czech Republic and the Supreme Audit Office of the Slovak Republic took place at the stage of preparation and implementation of the audit actions.
This exchange allowed a more thorough evaluation of the submitted documents and an analysis of the causes of the identified differences in the security of the asylum process.
This made it possible to focus on the crucial issues affecting the activities of asylum facilities.
Ultimately, this had a positive effect on the quality of the audit work carried out.
2.2 Summary of control results
The harmonisation of the asylum law of the Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic with European Union (EU) documents has been the subject of European Commission evaluation reports on the preparedness of both countries for accession to the EU.
Legislation in this area has continued since both countries joined the EU, with a view to further alignment with EU law.
The number of asylum seekers in the Czech Republic increased significantly in 2001.
In the Slovak Republic, the number of applicants has been on a steadily increasing trend, with a significant increase in applicants in 2001.
The Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic, within the meaning of international agreements, provide financial and material security to asylum seekers.
The national budget of both countries allocates the necessary funds for this purpose.
The amount of expenditure drawn from the national budget of both countries is influenced mainly by the number of applicants, the duration of the asylum procedure until the granting or non-granting of asylum, as well as the state of health of the applicants.
Some of the levies collected are comparable in both countries.
The Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic established asylum facilities in which they gradually created suitable conditions for the stay of the asylum seekers.
Asylum facilities shall provide asylum seekers with accommodation, meals, health care and basic material and hygiene equipment during their stay.
At the same time, free-time courses are organised and cultural and sporting opportunities are created for applicants.
Asylum facilities include reception and residence centres and, in the Czech Republic, integration centres.
The cost per day of stay depends on the availability and facilities of the centre and is comparable in both countries.
In the Czech Republic, the capacity of asylum facilities is basically stabilised.
In the Slovak Republic, it is expected to increase by almost 1 000 places in the next two years.
The aim is to create the conditions for applicants, a growing number of whom are expected to continue in the coming years in the context of the application of the Dublin Convention.
In the Czech Republic, the largest part of the approved budget expenditure was spent on the provision of services. The main expenditure under this heading was the cost of accommodation and meals for applicants in the asylum facilities operated under the contract, the cost of health care provided to asylum seekers and the cost of a permanent security service for the protection of asylum facilities.
Expenditure on salaries, payments for work carried out and insurance premiums also accounted for a significant share of total expenditure.
In the Slovak Republic, the budgetary management of the security of the migration policy and the asylum process was marked by many budgetary measures that addressed the justified demands related to the increase in the number of asylum seekers.
Expenditure on wages, salaries and insurance, expenditure on materials and services and expenditure on security services accounted for a significant share of current expenditure.
The results of the audit in the Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic highlighted some similar shortcomings that did not have a major impact on the financial management and management of state assets.
These were deficiencies in particular in the use of State Budget funds contrary to the purpose for which they were intended by the Act on the State Budget, where current expenditure covered actions having the character of technical evaluation of fixed tangible assets.
The Act on Accounting in Inventory and in Non-Compliance with Chart of Accounts was also violated.
In the Czech Republic, minor deficiencies were also found in the disposal of state property when it was passed on to another person without a contract.
Failure to comply with the deadline for reporting changes to the Land Registry data violated the Land Registry Act and leaving waste for disposal to a person who was not a person authorised to handle waste was a violation of the Waste Act.
Furthermore, in the Slovak Republic, deficiencies in the provision of unreasonably high advances for electricity and gas consumption, deficiencies in public procurement and in the management of state assets were found when the state's records were not sufficiently secured and the method of disposing of unneeded assets was not demonstrated.
The internal control system has been ensured in the Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic within the meaning of the generally binding legislation in force, which regulates the way financial control is carried out.
No deficiencies were found in the submitted written documentation.
The Supreme Audit Office, the Czech Republic and the Supreme Audit Office of the Slovak Republic, despite some shortcomings, evaluate positively the progress of SUZ and MU in providing services for asylum seekers.
Based on the findings of the audit, the Supreme Audit Office, the Czech Republic and the Supreme Audit Office of the Slovak Republic note that, despite the significant increase in the number of asylum seekers in recent years, the respective organisational units of both countries have managed the tasks related to the care of asylum seekers to an adequate level.
The evaluation shows that the system is functional and stable.
The results of the audit were discussed with interested statutory representatives of the relevant organisational units of the state on the Czech and Slovak sides.
Based on the results of the audit, we recommend that the positive methodological experience of the audit be used for further joint audit actions within the framework of international cooperation of supreme audit institutions.
3. Legislative framework
In both the Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic, the right of asylum is based on the 1951 Geneva Convention (Convention Relating to the Status of Refugees and the 1967 New York Protocol).
The first legislation was implemented by Act No. 498/1990 Coll., on Refugees.
In the Czech Republic, Act No. 325/1999 Coll., on Asylum and on the Amendment to Act No. 83/1991 Coll., on the Police of the Czech Republic, has been in force since 1 January 2000.
This Act has been amended a total of five times (by Act No 2/2002 Coll., Act No 217/2002 Coll., Act No 320/2002 Coll., Act No 519/2002 Coll., and Act No 222/2003 Coll.).
Two other amendments to Act No. 325/1999 Coll., on Asylum, are currently in the legislative process, and the adoption of further amendments will be linked to European legislation.
At the time of the creation of Act No 325/1999 Coll., on Asylum, the area of asylum was part of the 3rd pillar of the EU and asylum documents were political documents in terms of binding force.
Already during the adoption of this law, efforts were made to make it as compatible as possible with EU law.
A major change for asylum policy within the EU was the transfer of asylum issues from the 3rd to the 1st pillar, when the right of asylum became part of Community law from 1.5.1999 on the basis of the Treaty of Amsterdam.
This shift has triggered the need to create appropriate legislation, i.e. to create legal instruments with appropriate legal force.
The compatibility of Czech asylum law with EU documents has been the subject of regular evaluation reports by the European Commission.
Key Community regulations in the area of asylum were adopted shortly before or after the Czech Republic's accession to the EU and their transposition deadline had not yet expired at the time of the Supreme Audit Office's audit.
Once they have been implemented into Czech law, the Czech Republic will be obliged to notify the European Commission of how it has transposed them.
In the Slovak Republic, the procedure of the state authorities in the procedure for granting refugee status and determining the obligations of foreigners who have applied for refugee status or who have been granted temporary refuge in the territory of the Slovak Republic was laid down with effect from 1.1.1996 by the Act of the National Council of the Slovak Republic No 283/1995 Z. z., o utečencoch.
Since 2003, the Refugee Act has been replaced by Act No 480/2002 Coll. on Asylum and on Amending and Supplementing Certain Acts, incorporating the harmonisation of the legislation of the Slovak Republic with Council of Europe Directive 2001/55/EC of 20 July 2001 on minimum standards for providing temporary protection in the event of a mass influx of displaced persons and on measures to promote a balance of efforts between Member States in receiving and bearing the resulting consequences.
After two amendments to Act No. 480/2002 Coll., on Asylum, in 2003 and 2004, a third amendment was approved at the beginning of 2005, which means further harmonisation of the Slovak Republic's law with the EU legislation on migration and asylum.
Its important elements are, for example, the obligation for asylum-seekers to attend a course on the basics of the Slovak language during their stay in the integration centre, the designation of a guardian by the courts for underage foreigners if they reside in the territory of the Slovak Republic without a legal representative, the possibility for applicants to enter into an employment relationship under certain circumstances specified in the amendment to the Act.
The harmonisation of Slovak law with EU law on migration and the asylum process has been assessed in the European Commission's regular reports on the preparedness for the Slovak Republic's accession to the EU.
In 2002, a report highlighted the lack of staffing of the Ministry, which did not correspond to the increased number of asylum seekers.
4. Asylum process
In the Czech Republic, matters relating to refugees have been the responsibility of the Ministry of Interior since 1990.
With effect from 1 January 1996, the Ministry established the organisational unit of the State of SUZ. The reason was the separation of the state administration in the area of asylum procedure, which is carried out through the Department of Asylum and Migration Policy of the Ministry of Interior, from the provision of services to asylum seekers.
The mission of the SUZ is to provide accommodation, catering, health and other services to asylum seekers and asylum seekers for the Ministry.
In the Slovak Republic, the MU performs critical tasks in the area of the asylum process, in particular in terms of financial and material-technical security of the asylum process to the extent defined by specific regulations.
Priority tasks are issues related to the decision on applications of foreigners for asylum at the first level of administrative procedure and the complex operation of asylum facilities in the area of accommodation, catering, health, financial and material security of asylum seekers.
In addition, it prepares concepts on the migration policy of the state.
Cooperation and support from the Office of the United Nations High Commissioner for Refugees (UNHCR) has significantly influenced the results achieved in the development of the asylum system in the Slovak Republic in recent years.
A particular benefit was his financial contribution to the reconstruction of the housing stock for asylum seekers.
The UNHCR also co-financed social, cultural and sporting activities for applicants.
4.1 Evolution of the number of asylum seekers and the results of the asylum process
The development of the number of asylum seekers in the Czech Republic is shown in the graph.
There was a significant increase in 2001 due to the Kosovo crisis and an increase in the number of refugees from Chechnya in 2003.
An overview of the results of the asylum procedure in the Czech Republic is given in Table 1.
In total, 394 cases were granted asylum during the audited period, including 83 in 2001, 103 in 2002 and 208 in 2003.
A total of 22 731 asylum cases were not granted during the audited period and 22 636 asylum procedures were discontinued.
The number of parties to proceedings at 1.1. includes both persons who have not yet been the subject of a judgment and those who have not been the subject of a judgment.
It also includes persons who have been forgiven for missing the deadline for lodging an appeal, or who have had a previous decision annulled and have not yet been issued with a new one.
In the Slovak Republic, the number of asylum seekers is on a steadily increasing trend.
While there were 96 registered applicants in 1993, there were 10 358 in 2003.
There was a significant increase in the number of applicants in 2001, when 8 131 applicants were registered, which represented an increase of 423% compared to 2000, when 1 556 people applied for asylum.
Data on the results of the asylum procedure in the Slovak Republic during the audited period are presented in Table 2.
In the audited period, a total of 49 asylum applications were granted and their number did not exceed 0.22% of the registered number of applicants in each year.
Asylum was not granted in 860 cases.
Asylum procedures were stopped in a total of 23 088 cases.
By staying outside the asylum facility without a permit, the majority of applicants voluntarily left the asylum procedure before it was completed.
In 2002 it was more than 82% of the total number of registered applicants and in 2003 it was more than 94% of persons.
The duration of an applicant's stay in an asylum facility, converted into an average number of days, was 13 days in 2001, 18 days in 2002 and 19 days in 2003.
The findings of the Ministry also show that the majority of applicants entering the asylum process in the Slovak Republic are seeking better living conditions, i.e. so-called economic migration, which has no basis in the documents adopted by the international community, to which the Slovak Republic has also subscribed.
In these cases, the asylum system is used by the applicants for a specific purpose.
An application for asylum based on economic reasons, such as seeking employment or better living conditions, is not a ground for granting asylum.
Such an application should, in accordance with the provisions of Article 12(1)(a) of Law No 480/2002 on asylum, be considered to be manifestly unfounded and be decided within 30 days.
There are still no internationally recognised principles for identifying economic migrants to distinguish them from those persecuted in their country of origin and to speed up the asylum process.
In the light of the above, it can be concluded that the Slovak Republic is a transit country in terms of international migration and the countries of destination for migration were and remain Western European countries.
Upon accession to the EU, the Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic acceded to the Agreement determining the State responsible for examining asylum applications lodged in one of the Member States of the European Communities (Dublin Convention).
The Convention prevents multiple ad hoc asylum applications from being made in more than one EU country.
Applicants return to the country of first registration after being positively identified in EURODAC.
As the Slovak Republic is part of the external border of EU countries, the application of the Dublin Convention may in the next period result in an increase in the number of applicants who will need to create conditions to stay in the Slovak Republic pending a decision on whether or not to grant asylum.
4.2 Implementation of funds
The Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic are obliged to provide financial and material security to asylum seekers in accordance with international agreements and the resulting national legislation.
The national budget of both countries allocates the necessary funds for this purpose.
The amount of expenditure drawn from the state budget is mainly influenced by the number of applicants, the duration of the asylum procedure until the final decision on the granting of asylum, as well as their state of health.
The increasing number of people entering the asylum process has a direct impact on the increased uptake of statutory benefits for applicants, such as food, pocket money, expenditure on basic material equipment, other material equipment and other social contributions.
Some of the levies collected are comparable in both countries.
In the Czech Republic during the audited period, the meal allowance was CZK 58 per person per day, the allowance was CZK 12 per person per day and CZK 6 per child up to the age of 15.
The average cost per asylum seeker per stay day was CZK 351 in 2001, CZK 383 in 2002 and CZK 345 in 2003.
In addition to pocket money, an allowance of up to the minimum subsistence level may be granted to asylum seekers accommodated in self-catering accommodation centres and to applicants accommodated in private accommodation outside asylum facilities.
Act No 325/1999 Coll., on Asylum, as amended, inter alia grants municipalities the right to a contribution to cover the costs incurred in connection with an asylum facility on their territory in Section 84.
The amount of the allowance was determined annually by government resolutions and in the audited period it amounted to CZK 7 per person per stay day.
At the same time, municipalities are entitled to a contribution to cover the non-investment costs of the primary schools attended by asylum seekers.
The amount of the allowance was CZK 120 per pupil per month in the audited period, provided that the person attended the school for at least 10 days in that month.
In the Slovak Republic, during the audited period, the meal allowance was SKK 70 per person per day, the allowance was SKK 12 per person per day and SKK 8 per child up to 15 years of age.
The cost per asylum seeker per stay day was on average SKK 442 in 2001, SKK 447 in 2002 and SKK 445 in 2003.
Municipalities in whose territory an asylum facility is located are granted a contribution to cover part of the costs incurred by the municipality in connection with the establishment and operation of the asylum facility, in accordance with Act No 480/2002 Coll., on Asylum.
Municipalities have so far made only partial use of the possibility to apply for the allowance, which is set at SKK 1 500 per bed per calendar year.
National legislation in both countries provides for a 90-day period from the initiation of the asylum procedure to issue an asylum decision, while allowing for an extension of that period in justified cases.
The most common reason for extending the asylum procedure, which affects the level of state budget expenditure, is the lodging of appeals against the decision not to grant asylum.
In the Czech Republic, the highest number of final decisions was delivered between 90 and 180 days (31.54%).
The second highest number was within 90 days of the date of the asylum application (17.61%).
Overall, 73% of the asylum procedures opened in 2001-2003 were finally decided within one year of their opening.
Within two years of the opening of the asylum procedure, 92% of asylum applications were finally decided.
In the Slovak Republic, according to the Ministry of Justice, up to 90 % of applicants who have not been granted asylum make use of the legal possibility to extend the deadline by lodging an appeal with a court.
In some cases it was extended by more than a year and the court proceedings were not concluded at the time of the inspection.
Some benefit in shortening the asylum procedure is expected from the establishment of joint workplaces of the Ministry and the Office of the Border and Cudzinecke Police Force in the reception centres in Adam-Gbeloch, Opatovský Novy. Liptovský Vlachoch, which was taken at the time of the execution of the inspection since 1.10.2004.
This is intended to simplify procedures and shorten the time limits for procedures between the two components of the asylum process.
5. Asylum facilities
The Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic established asylum facilities in which they gradually created suitable conditions for the provision of services to participants in the asylum procedure.
Asylum facilities provide asylum seekers with accommodation, meals, health care, pocket money and basic material and sanitary supplies during their stay.
At the same time, courses are organised as part of leisure activities and the conditions for cultural and sporting activities are created for applicants.
5.1 Asylum facilities in the Czech Republic
SUZ manages and operates asylum facilities and is responsible for managing state property in the premises of the reception centre Vyšní Lhota, reception and residence centre Červený Újezd, residential centres Bělá-Jezová, Zastávka u Brna, Kostelec nad Orlicí, Havířov and in the premises of the integration asylum centres Jaroměř and Zastávka u Brna.
At the beginning of 2002, a reception centre was established in the transit area of Prague-Ruzyně Airport.
SUZ also operates asylum facilities in Stráž pod Ralskem, Seč, Bruntál, Zbýšov and Kashav, which are operated by a legal entity on the basis of Section 80(3) of Act No 325/1999 Coll., on Asylum.
The total accommodation capacity of the reception centres is 747 places and the total accommodation capacity of the residential centres is 1 977 places.
Reception centres are closed facilities that asylum seekers are not allowed to leave until the prescribed medical examinations and basic reception operations referred to in Act No 325/1999 Coll., on Asylum have been carried out.
Failure to comply with the obligation to stay in a reception centre is considered a misdemeanour.
The accommodation centres to which asylum seekers arrive from reception centres shall be used to accommodate asylum seekers until the decision on their application or non-application becomes final.
Under certain conditions, asylum seekers have the possibility to leave or stay in private.
Asylum seekers have the right to free accommodation, food, other services and pocket money in the reception and residence centres in which they are registered for residence, provided that they have taken the necessary steps to ascertain their financial and financial situation, and that situation is such that they cannot reasonably be required to pay part of those services.
Integration asylum centres shall serve as temporary accommodation for persons who have been granted asylum.
Clients are accommodated in the centre on the basis of an accommodation contract, which is usually concluded for 6 months with the possibility of extension until they can be provided with adequate housing or they can arrange it themselves within the framework of the state integration programme.
In the integration centre, clients pay all accommodation costs and eat individually.
Persons with recognised refugee status have the same rights and obligations as Czech citizens and have the same access to social security.
5.1.1 Costs of asylum facilities
For the comparison of asylum facilities, it was chosen as the best indicator of the amount of costs per physical stay day.
It is based on the actual number of persons to whom services provided by the asylum facility have actually been provided on a daily basis.
In Table 3 and in the graph, the facilities operated by SUZs are designated as own and those operated by legal entities on the basis of Section 80 of Act No 325/1999 Coll., on Asylum, as contractual.
The cost of one day's stay at the SUZ's own asylum facility decreases with the increase in the number of such facilities. This is due to the fact that the fixed costs of running the premises are allocated to more asylum seekers.
The total cost of providing services to asylum seekers increases in direct proportion as the number of asylum seekers increases.
In 2001 and 2003, asylum seekers in crisis capacity were accommodated in their own asylum facilities.
In part of 2003, extra accommodation capacity in residential containers (320 beds in total) was even used to deal with the capacity crisis in asylum facilities caused by the mass arrival of asylum seekers of Chechen nationality from Poland.
This capacity was provided by renting 80 residential and 6 sanitary containers.
Expenditure per applicant per day of stay was lower in these years.
However, it is not possible to maintain a high level of capacity in these facilities in the long term.
Account should be taken of the risks of an unexpected influx of refugees and the associated requirement for increased accommodation capacity.
At the same time, it is not possible to carry out certain repairs, maintenance and investments when the accommodation capacity is fully occupied.
The form of the asylum facilities operated under contract allows for the adoption of operational solutions in the area of the capacity of the asylum facilities, where necessary.
5.2 Asylum facilities in the Slovak Republic
Asylum facilities are reception centres and residence centres.
The Ministry has in its administration the reception centres Adamov-Gbely, Rohovce, Opatovská Nová Ves and the residential centre Brezová pod Bradlom.
The total capacity of the reception centres is 470 places and the accommodation centre 100 places.
The growing number of asylum seekers was dealt with by the Ministry by setting up a residential centre in Gabčíkov in the rented premises of the Slovak Technical University of Bratislava with a flexible available capacity of 250 to 940 places.
According to the contract concluded, the lessor also ensures the provision of services related to the operation of the centre.
In this form, MÚ solved the lack of its own staff.
The residential centre was also temporarily established in 2002 and 2003 in rented premises in Jazierciach with a capacity of 160 places.
Admission centers had an average occupancy rate of 57% to 62% during the audited period, while residential centers had an average occupancy rate of 33% to 50%.
Occupancy varied with the season and was highest in the last months of the year, when many more applicants applied for asylum compared to other months of the year.
Further increase of accommodation capacity by about 450 places will be possible after the reconstruction of the resorts in Opatovská, Nové Vsi and Vlachoch.
With the completion of the centre in Humennom in 2006, the number of seats will increase by a further 550 persons.
5.2.1 Costs of asylum facilities
Data on the costs of running asylum facilities are given in Table 4.
Their analysis shows that the operation of an asylum facility by another entity providing premises and ensuring the operation of the facility, including the related activities, is one of the options for safeguarding, at a reasonable cost, the tasks resulting from the asylum seekers’ stay in the Slovak Republic.
6. Management of State funds and property
6.1 Economic analysis
In the Czech Republic, the approved budget expenditure of SUZ was increased by CZK 103,077 thousand by thirteen budget measures in 2001.
Of this, CZK 89,979,000 (87.29%) were earmarked funds. The bulk of it was intended to deal with the consequences of the Kosovo crisis (CZK 44,538,000) and to provide health care to asylum seekers (CZK 38,870,000).
In 2002, SUZ expenditure was increased by CZK 88,362,000 by twenty budget measures.
Of this, CZK 62,282,000 (70.48%) were earmarked funds, the bulk of which (CZK 53,200,000) was intended for health care provided to asylum seekers.
In 2003, the expenditure of SUZ was increased by fourteen budgetary measures by a total of CZK 88,078,000.
Of this, CZK 82,000,000 (93.09%) were earmarked funds released from the General Treasury Administration.
The amount of CZK 51,000,000 was intended for health care provided to asylum seekers, CZK 24,000,000 for dealing with the influx of Chechen refugees, and CZK 7,000,000 for the humanitarian evacuation program of disabled residents of the Republic of Iraq.
An overview of the execution of the selected SUZ expenditure in the audited period is given in Table 5.
It is clear from the overview above that the largest part of the expenditure was spent on the provision of services.
Expenditure on purchasing services ranged from 37.07% to 38.23% of total expenditure.
Expenditure on accommodation and catering for applicants in contract-based asylum facilities, expenditure on medical care provided to asylum seekers and the cost of a permanent security service for guarding individual asylum facilities were inflammatory expenditure under this heading.
Expenditure on salaries, payments for work done and insurance premiums (23.99% - 24.97%) accounted for a significant share of the total SUZ expenditure.
Last but not least, purchases of materials accounted for a significant share of total SUZ expenditure (around 14%), with the largest share of this expenditure coming from food purchases (7.61%? 9.83% of total expenditure).
In the Slovak Republic, the budget management of the Ministry for the security of the migration policy and asylum process was marked by more budgetary measures, which addressed justified demands related to the increase in the number of asylum seekers.
The draft and budget breakdown in the expenditure section was characterised, in particular in 2001 and 2002, by an imbalance between the requirements of the MA, which were based on the increase in the number of asylum seekers and the need to finance their legal entitlements, and the resources provided, in particular in the main category of current expenditure.
The budget chapter administrator addressed the disproportion with several budgetary measures during the year, increasing the current expenditure limit by 41.8% in 2001 and by 71.7% in 2002.
An overview of the implementation of selected MU expenditure in the audited period is given in Table 6.
Expenditure on wages, salaries and insurance premiums in the range of 24.47% to 30.51% and expenditure on materials and services in the range of 21.04% to 38.82% of total expenditure contributed decisively to the absorption of current expenditure.
More than 10% was spent on security services and between 3.92% and 7.20% on food purchases.
Another significant part was expenditure on accommodation and catering services, ranging from 10.82% to 26.96%.
Capital expenditure accounted for a relatively low share, ranging from 1.03% to 6.25%.
6.2 Weaknesses identified
The results of the audit in the Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic pointed to some similar deficiencies which did not have a major impact on the management of state funds and assets.
These were deficiencies in particular in the following areas: (b) infringement of the Accounting Act in the area of inventories and non-compliance with the indicative chart of accounts.
In the Czech Republic, minor deficiencies in the disposal of state property were also found when it was passed on to another person without a proper contract; failure to comply with the deadline for reporting changes to the Land Registry data violated the Land Registry Act; Leaving the waste for disposal to a person who was not the person authorised to dispose of the waste was in breach of the Waste Act.
In the Slovak Republic, deficiencies in the provision of unreasonably high advances for the consumption of electricity and natural gas, deficiencies in public procurement and in the management of the state's assets were found when the state's records were not sufficiently secured and the method of disposing of unneeded assets was not demonstrated.
7. Internal control system
In the Czech Republic, an internal control system within the meaning of the provisions of Act No.320/2001 Coll., on financial control, defining the competencies of individual employees responsible for carrying out preliminary, ongoing and subsequent control, was introduced at SUZ on 1 July 2002.
At the same time, an internal audit system was put in place and an internal auditor of the SUZ was appointed.
Each year, SUZs are issued with a plan of financial, social and operational technical controls aimed at a comprehensive verification of the management of state funds and property in individual asylum facilities managed by SUZs.
42 management checks were carried out in 2002 and 54 in 2003.
In the Slovak Republic, the Ministry of the Interior of the Slovak Republic directly performs the audit activities in the departments and organisations of the Ministry of the Interior with regard to compliance with generally binding legal regulations and internal regulations.
No checks were carried out on the performance of tasks in the field of asylum policy during the audited period.
The internal control system of the Ministry of Finance is focused mainly on the implementation of preliminary and continuous financial control according to the Act No. 502/2001 Coll., on financial control and internal audit.
The performance of financial control is also regulated by internal regulations of the Director of the Ministry.
The substantive focus of the audit activity as part of the management of individual departments was established in the framework of the MU's annual plan.
Union directors have been designated as responsible for exercising control in this area.
According to the submitted written documentation, no deficiencies were found.
Since the entry into force of Decree No 28 of 23 April 2004 of the Ministry of Interior of the Slovak Republic, the Ministry of Interior has been obliged to handle and investigate complaints under the Complaints Act.
The organizational security of this task will need to be resolved in the near future.
Communiqué
This report was drawn up in the Czech, English and Slovak languages and signed by the Vice-President of the Supreme Audit Office and the President of the Supreme Audit Office of the Slovak Republic.
Joint Report on Parallel Checks on Motorway Construction Expenditure Prague – Dresden
Opening Word
Without trans-European transport networks, a modern, dynamic and, above all, united Europe would be hard to imagine.
Quality transport networks are a prerequisite for maintaining and raising the standard of living of Europeans.
Creating the conditions for fast, safe, reliable and cheap transport of people and goods is an important task for the state administration.
It is a very demanding task both in organisational and technical terms and in financial terms.
European efforts at sustainable mobility often find it difficult to compromise with Europe's interest in maintaining a healthy and human-friendly environment.
The construction of transport infrastructure, also thanks to the necessary measures for the benefit of the environment, is financially demanding.
Considerate procedures and decision-making by the state administration in the preparation and acquisition of transport constructions can bring significant savings in public financial resources that can be used for the benefit of citizens in other ways.
One of the roles of audit institutions is to actively identify deficiencies that have led to lower than achievable economy, efficiency and effectiveness of expenditure on transport infrastructure construction.
The audit institutions should not only alert the public administration to the shortcomings identified, but recommend measures to improve the situation, for the benefit of citizens, for the benefit of Europe.
Short Summary
The two supreme audit institutions of the Federal Republic of Germany and the Czech Republic (hereinafter referred to as Germany and the Czech Republic), the Bundesrechnungshof (hereinafter referred to as BRH) and the Supreme Audit Office (hereinafter referred to as the SAO), carried out parallel audits of expenditure on the construction of the Prague-Dresden motorway (motorway D 8 in the Czech Republic and motorway A 17 in Germany) in 2005 and 2006.
The motorways are connected by a border bridge co-financed by both countries.
Thanks to cooperation with the SAO, BRH was able to carry out a thorough check of the effectiveness of the funds spent on the construction of the border bridge.
Based on the close cooperation with BRH, the SAO obtained the necessary data on the German costs of bridges and tunnels for comparative purposes.
The Czech Republic and the Federal Republic of Germany concluded a contract for the construction of a border bridge in 2000.
According to the construction process, the completion date was finally scheduled for the end of 2006.
However, in the course of awarding the contract, the Road and Motorway Directorate of the Czech Republic (hereinafter referred to as the RMD) made errors which led to an extension of the competition period and a shortening of the construction period.
The contract with the contractor was therefore signed later than expected.
The contractor agreed to build the bridge in a shorter time and ensure its completion in 2006.
As a result, however, the RMD had to accept a significant increase in the price of the bridge.
As part of the joint inspection work, the prices of bridges on the D 8 and A 17 motorways were compared on the basis of the conversion per square metre of the bridge construction area.
Statistical analysis of prices made it possible to identify bridges with high costs per square metre of area.
Analysis revealed that the border bridge was the most expensive bridge in the group of large bridges with a length of over 100 meters.
The price increase could also be attributed to the delay in the procurement procedure.
When analysing the tunnel construction costs, the auditors found that the tunnel prices on the D8 motorway were significantly higher than those of comparable tunnels in Germany.
BRH and the SAO now have a database of prices of bridges and tunnels on which they will be able to rely in future checks on the effectiveness of the funds used for transport infrastructure purposes.
For the construction of bridges, both audit institutions recommend: standardization of bridge structures and use of combined steel-concrete and special structures only in cases where prestressed reinforced concrete bridges are not adequate.
The main features of the collaboration were its team spirit and informality.
During the parallel inspection, the auditors shared their specific knowledge and skills.
Introduction
This report informs about the progress and results of parallel inspections of funds spent on the construction of a motorway connecting Dresden and Prague (D 8 in the Czech Republic and A 17 in Germany).
The audits focused on the economy, efficiency and effectiveness of the expenditure incurred.
Parallel audits were carried out by BRH in Germany and SAO in the Czech Republic.
Parallel inspections took place in 2005 and 2006.
The SAO audited the economy, efficiency and effectiveness of expenditure amounting to approximately CZK 20 billion including VAT (approximately EUR 690 million) on the border section of the D8 motorway, which has the number 0807.
Of this amount, € 64 million from European funds (ISPA) were planned to be used.
In addition to a parallel audit carried out jointly with the SAO, the BRH audited the preparation and construction of the A17 motorway in 2003 and 2005.
The funds spent on the construction of all three sections of the A17 motorway amounted to around €670 million, of which €277 million will be co-financed by European funds (EFRE).
The motorway from Prague to Dresden is part of the Trans-European Transport Corridor IV. Dresden – Central and Southern parts of Eastern Europe – Istanbul.
The D8 motorway runs from Prague through Ústí nad Labem to the state border.
The A17 motorway runs from the Czech Republic/Germany border to Dresden.
At the time of the inspection, the border sections of both motorways were under construction.
These sections were put into operation on 21 December 2006.
On the German side, the motorway is finished until Dresden, while on the Czech side of the D8 motorway it is not finished, because the section No. 0805 of 16.4 km through the Bohemian Central Mountains between Řehlovice and Lovosicemi is still in the preparatory phase.
According to the plan, section No.0805 between Prague and Ústí nad Labem is to pass through the protected landscape area of the Bohemian Central Mountains.
According to forecasts made in the Czech Republic in 2001, the traffic intensity on the border section No 0807 of the D 8 motorway should be 22 940 vehicles per day in 2008.
According to German forecasts, traffic at the Czech border will total about 25,000 vehicles per day in 2010.
In 2000, Germany and the Czech Republic concluded an agreement between the Federal Republic of Germany and the Czech Republic on the interconnection of the German motorway A 17 and the Czech motorway D 8 at the common state border by the construction of a border bridge.
Both countries are involved in the construction of the bridge, so the control of the construction of this bridge was an important aspect of the parallel checks carried out by both control institutions.
Cooperation between BRH and SAO
Since mid-2005, the two supreme audit institutions have been discussing close cooperation in the inspection of the construction of the motorway connecting Prague and Dresden.
Both sides had serious reasons for such cooperation:
1.
Germany participates financially in the construction of the border bridge.
Its planning and construction rests on the RMD.
Therefore, BRH itself did not have the opportunity to carry out an inspection of that construction project.
2.
The SAO did not have the comparative database needed to assess whether the funds were used economically in terms of the overall level of costs for bridges and tunnels on D 8.
3.
The SAO needed to make sure that the expenditures were effective, taking into account the main technical parameters of the motorway, especially the arrangement of bridges and tunnels, which should allow the same quality of travel on both sides of the border.
Following preparatory negotiations, an Agreement on the coordinated control of the D 8/A 17 motorway was signed by the presidents of the two supreme audit institutions on 4 January 2006.
The joint working meetings of the auditors of BRH and SAO took place during the whole year 2006 at an interval of approximately six to eight weeks.
The meetings addressed current problems and tasks, in particular the preparation of a uniform methodology for the control of the prices of bridges and tunnels, the exchange of findings from the control of the construction of the border bridge and the preparation of joint reports.
The continuous exchange of information was an important sign of cooperation.
While BRH carried out most of the work on comparing technical parameters of bridges and tunnels and tendering procedures, the SAO prepared a methodology for controlling the prices of bridges and tunnels.
The applicable legal standards for government control differ between the two countries in the way control actions are closed.
For these reasons, the two SAIs drew their own audit conclusions on their findings during their inspections.
This report reflects the results achieved by the parallel controls.
The cooperation will be concluded by the signing of the final communiqué in the first half of 2007.
Border bridge construction
In a contract of 2000, the Czech Republic and the Federal Republic of Germany agreed on a date of completion of the construction of the border bridge in 2005, as far as possible.
The two parties then agreed to postpone the deadline until the end of 2006.
The RMD acted as an investor.
Germany accounts for 42.13% of the construction of the bridge.
In the period up to 1999, the RMD proposed that the border bridge should have a prestressed reinforced concrete structure.
In 1999, in agreement with its partners in Germany, the RMD agreed to a coupled steel-concrete structure.
This decision was justified by the reduction of the number of double pillars from seven to five and the consequent environmental benefits of such a solution for the valley.
In addition, the comparative calculations carried out by the RMD indicated that the steel-concrete coupled construction should be a less costly option.
But there was an error in the calculations.
They stated that the new bonded steel-concrete structure would be only 397 m long.
It's actually 5% longer.
During the award of the contract for the border bridge, the RMD made serious errors.
It did not prepare procurement documents adequately describing the assessment criteria and sub-criteria for the comparison of tenders.
This error resulted in the evaluation and evaluation committee and the Director-General evaluating the tenders in a subjective and problematic manner.
The unsuccessful tenderer contested the results of the procurement procedure.
A subsequent investigation by the Office for the Protection of Competition led to the conclusion of the contract for the award of the works contract later than originally envisaged.
This meant that the 2004 summer construction season, after evaluation of the bids, was wasted and the time allocated for construction was shortened.
The construction company agreed to build the bridge in a shorter time, but at the cost of significantly increasing costs.
The procedure offered included the double installation of the bridge superstructure, a change in the structure of the pillars and the installation and fitting-out of the assembly areas.
In addition, the construction company was not able to procure the originally designed type of steel in due time.
The new type of steel – at a cost of about €4 million, which had to be paid in full by the RMD – made it possible to reduce the time taken to implement the corrosion protection, which in turn made it possible to reduce the overall construction time of the border bridge.
Compared to the net price of €18.3 million offered in the tender, the total cost of the construction of the border bridge will increase significantly as a result of the accelerated procedure and other modifications in the funding.
The reduced time allocated to construction work resulted in a price increase of around €5.9 million.
Currently, the net cost is around €25.9 million, not taking into account the automatic price increase (as of October 2006).
Comparison of bridge and tunnel prices
The two supreme audit institutions analysed the prices of bridges on the D8 and A17 motorways in order to identify bridges whose price was too high and to compare the prices of bridges on the German and Czech sides.
The analysis was based on methods of mathematical statistics.
