Furthermore, tunnels on the D8 were compared with similar tunnels on motorways in Germany (A 71, A 38 and A 4).
There are no similar tunnels on the A-17.
In preparing this part of the inspection, it was important to identify precisely the relevant prices.
The two supreme audit institutions have established the following principles for the comparison of bridge prices: based on the actual price paid for the bridge excluding value added tax (VAT rates differ in the Czech Republic and the Slovak Republic and are subject to changes); do not take into account prices for special bases of the bridge; convert prices in CZK into € according to the European Central Bank rate valid on 30.12.2005 (CZK 29 = € 1).
On both D 8 and A 17 there are bridges of various constructions (steel-concrete, prestressed reinforced concrete, bonded steel-concrete and steel) and of different sizes.
In order to ensure the homogeneity of the studied structures, the bridges were divided into groups according to the type of construction.
Within these groups, the size of the bridge was also taken into account in the analyses.
Concrete bridges were by far the most common type of construction on both sides of the border (20 bridges on the D8, 36 bridges on the A17).
The analysis focused mainly on this group of bridges, as the numerical size of the set allowed the use of mathematical and statistical methods with a high degree of reliability of the results.
The inspections found that the prices per m 2 of concrete bridges on both D 8 and A 17 depend on the size of the bridge (its length, area): the larger the bridge, the lower the price per m2 of bridge construction.
For German bridges, the price dependence on size was stronger than for Czech bridges (see graphs below).
Two concrete bridges on the A 17 and four on the D 8 have been identified and their price is significantly higher.
In order to find these bridges, a method was used that allowed to find atypical values in the set of numbers, and a regression analysis that took into account the dependence of the price of the bridge on its size (for the determination of atypically expensive bridges, the distance from the regression curve upwards is decisive).
However, information on the atypically high price of those bridges should only be considered as an indicator of which bridges should be targeted.
In order to draw a generally applicable conclusion, the causes of the high price in each case identified should be analysed.
One such cause may be inefficient administrative measures, or environmental protection requirements or any other plausible reason.
Given that the Czech concrete bridges are on average longer than the German ones (see table below), the proven negative dependence of the price of the bridge on its length could lead to the assumption that the Czech bridges should be cheaper.
The hypothesis of lower prices of Czech concrete bridges is also supported by lower labour costs in the construction sector in the Czech Republic compared to Germany.
On the contrary, the results of the analysis show that the prices of concrete bridges on D 8 are higher than on A 17.
In the course of the joint discussions, two possible causes have been identified:
1.
In Germany, most bridges are awarded as separate contracts.
Such an award allows small and medium-sized regional firms to compete for the contract.
In the Czech Republic, many bridges are awarded as part of a contract for a part of the motorway route, which is several kilometres long.
Small and medium-sized companies do not participate in such a competition because the contract is too big for them.
2.
There is more variability in bridge construction in the Czech Republic than in Germany.
In this context, the SAO found that the type of bridge construction was decided long before its construction.
The decision is taken at the time when the application for a territorial decision is submitted to the RMD.
Although the RMD selects types of bridges from several variants at this time, economy plays a smaller role in the decision-making process.
A general conclusion on the prices of bridges in the Czech Republic and Germany could only be reached on the basis of a thorough analysis of the construction works market in both countries, which would, however, go beyond the scope of this control action.
The boundary bridge is the most expensive bonded steel concrete bridge on D 8 and A 17.
In the case of tunnels, it was found that tunnel prices on D 8 are twice as high as tunnels in Germany.
However, a comparison of the main construction-technical parameters of the tunnels on the D8 with the tunnels in Germany did not show any significant difference.
Significant price differences could be attributed to the fact that there is no real competition in the tunnel construction industry in the Czech Republic.
However, the small number of tunnels did not allow for any generally applicable conclusions.
Effectiveness and efficiency of the construction of the Dresden-Prague motorway
A separate Czech Republic was established in 1993.
In the same year, the government approved the concept of developing a motorway network.
The D8 motorway was to be gradually extended from Prague to the state border with Germany.
Section No. 0805 through the Bohemian Central Mountains should have been acquired before the border section No. 0807.
The Czech Republic acquires the D8 motorway as part of the programme financing.
In programme no. 327 220 „Construction of the D8 motorway Prague – Ústí nad Labem – state border of the Czech Republic/Germany” two objectives of the construction of the D8 motorway were set:
1.
to improve the connection between Prague and Ústí nad Labem – a national goal;
2.
Improve the connection between Prague and Berlin within the framework of the IV Trans-European Transport Corridor Berlin – Istanbul – an international objective.
According to the programme, the construction of the section leading through České středohoří should have been completed in June 2008 and the construction of the border section No. 0807 in June 2007.
It should be added here that the date of completion is not identical to the date of commissioning.
The SAO found that the preparation of the construction of the section leading through the Czech Central Mountains is now in the stage of an official zoning decision.
Environmental activists challenged the decision with lawsuits.
No final judgment is given on the actions.
Therefore, it cannot be excluded that the section leading through České středohoří will be completed several years later than indicated in the programme documentation.
In the summer of 2006, the Czech Ministry of Transport proposed to postpone the completion date of section 0805 to 2010.
As a result of these facts, the construction of the border section did not achieve the national objective of improving the connection between Prague and Ústí nad Labem.
The RMD carried out an estimate of capital expenditure for the border section D 8 No 0807 and the section No 0805 through the Bohemian Central Mountains in the years 1999-2001.
The estimated capital expenditure included in the investment costs amounts to about €639 million in total for both sections 0805 and 0807.
Table 2: Results of the economic assessment (EIRR)
All the motorways and expressways under consideration were opened, both sections of the D8 were opened in 2007.
On the northern part of the border section, the traffic intensity of 18,000 vehicles per day in 2010
All the motorways and expressways under consideration were opened, both sections of the D8 were opened in 2007.
On the northern part of the border section, the traffic intensity of 22,000 vehicles per day in 2010
All the motorways and expressways under consideration were opened, both sections of the D8 were opened in 2007.
On the northern part of the border section, the traffic intensity of 25,000 vehicles per day in 2010
The economic analysis carried out in 2001 was based on the assumption that the border section and the section through the Czech Central Mountains would be completed in time.
At the time of the inspection, however, it is clear that the section through the Bohemian Central Mountains will not be opened at the original date.
This will reduce the above EIRRs.
The cost of both sections, net of VAT, as at 31.12.2005, was €897 million, i.e. 27.4% higher than the cost level used in the economic analysis in 2001.
However, there is a risk that section 0805 will be run on a different, more costly route, which would affect the above-mentioned economic viability threshold.
Economic analysis has shown that the construction of both sections of the motorway is economically viable with a traffic intensity of at least 18 000 vehicles per day in 2010.
The forecast for 2008 amounted to 22 940 vehicles, but the study dealing with the issue of bypass routes for the missing section through the Czech Central Mountains counts on the traffic intensity of 12 600 to 13 750 vehicles per day, which pushes the investment below the economic viability threshold.
With a higher load on bypass routes for the missing section through České středohoří, there is a high probability of congestion and thus an increase in external costs, which were not foreseen in the original economic analysis.
The above mentioned reduction in revenues and increase in costs means that the already low EIRR that the construction of the border section D 8 should have had will not be achieved.
Construction of the D 8 border section is therefore inefficient.
The construction of the A 17 motorway also involved similar cost overruns as the two sections of the D 8.
As part of the overall economic assessment of the 1992 Federal Road Development Plan for long-distance transport, the Federal Ministry of Transport of Germany calculated a cost of about €320 million.
The resulting cost-benefit ratio was 4.6.
In the meantime, however, costs have risen sharply to about €670 million, reducing the cost-benefit ratio by more than half.
In addition to a parallel audit carried out jointly with the SAO, the BRH audited the preparation and construction of the A17 motorway in 2003 and 2005.
The BRH found that the competent State approval authority had requested an off-road tunnel for the third section of A17, which was not needed.
During the inspection, BRH managed to convince the Federal Ministry and the German Road Construction Administration that the tunnel was not necessary.
This resulted in savings of around €10 million in construction costs alone and €0.15 million in annual operating costs.
In its assessment of Section A 17, the BRH found that the construction project had not been properly prepared.
This is illustrated by the following example:
At the preparatory stage, the Road Construction Administration did not investigate the bedrock conditions in the Dölzschen rocks because the site was difficult to access.
This was done after the tunnel excavator hit the rock.
Tests have shown that the rocks inside are more weathered than anticipated during the selection process.
Costly measures such as anchoring, grouting and protective nets against falling rocks had to be taken, costing around €3.3 million.
The Road and Motorway Directorate had to bear the costs not only of other emergency operations, such as security measures in the rocks of Dölzschen, but also of measures that would not have been necessary if the construction project had been properly prepared first.
Expenditure of this kind includes costs related to interruption of construction operations (€3.2 million), additional training of drilling personnel (€0.5 million) and rising wages and expenditure on goods due to project duration overruns (€7 million).
The cost overrun thus amounted to around €10.7 million, which did not correspond to any added value.
On the basis of the lessons learned from this and other audited projects, BRH asked the Federal Ministry to apply stricter guidance in its supervision of road construction works.
In doing so, the Federal Ministry should give the Road and Motorway Directorates the necessary impetus to introduce quality assurance systems.
Benefits of cooperation for BRH and SAO Cooperation fulfilled the intention of both audit institutions.
BRH gained control knowledge about the preparation, commissioning and progress of the construction of the border bridge.
The two supreme audit institutions have built up a database of bridge and tunnel prices on which future controls can be based.
The SAO found that the main construction and technical parameters of bridges, tunnels and the route of the D 8 motorway did not differ from those of the A 17.
The auditors of the two institutions involved in the cooperation gained new experience which they can use in further inspections.

Coordinated control of funds earmarked for financing projects implemented under the Operational Programme for Cross-border Cooperation Czech Republic – Republic of Poland 2007–2013
INTRODUCTION
Europe's developing community is a driving force behind cross-border cooperation as an integral part of the wider territorial cooperation that has been part of our lives for the last 20 years.
So let this community give an introduction to this joint report by the two supreme audit institutions of the neighbouring countries:
"185 million EU citizens (or 37% of the population) live in border areas.
These areas are usually underdeveloped and peripheral territories, often a remnant of the darker parts of Europe's history.
The main objective of cross-border cooperation is to help alleviate the existence of borders as administrative, legal and physical barriers and further help develop the dormant potential of border regions.
The programmes cover different border regions: some cover sea basins, others inland borders within the EU or borders shared with candidate countries.
Most of the programmes are bilateral, but some include more than two neighbouring countries. “
The programmes run for seven years and receive a total of €5.7 billion from the EU budget.
‘The programmes include the following activities: repairing and rebuilding cross-border roads, cycle paths and bridges, investing in cross-border waste management systems, health facilities, research centres, flood control measures, etc., joint management of natural sites or development of tourist destinations, development of common services for the local population, advice on employment issues, creation of thematic networks and clusters for innovation.
Support from the programme is provided on condition that organisations from both sides of the border (e.g. regional authorities, universities or SMEs) come together to implement projects based on the needs of the border region.
Representatives of cooperating regions, relevant ministries and other local partners meet regularly to decide which projects will be supported.
This system should lead to a sustainable form of cooperation even after the programme itself has ended.
The funding is provided on condition that project partners from both sides of the border cooperate in a way that fulfils at least two of the following four elements: joint preparation, joint implementation, joint staff, joint financing. “
Under the partnership, the tasks and responsibilities for the preparation, implementation, financing and control of project activities are clearly defined and distributed to the relevant partners.
The main attributes of the partnership are: joint selection of the lead partner, joint preparation of the project and the application, conclusion of a joint partnership agreement signed by all partners, assumption of overall responsibility for the implementation of the project by the lead partner, total support payments received by the lead partner and transferred to the relevant project partners.
Eligible projects implemented on the territory of regions and voivodships adjacent to the common border may be supported.
OBJECTIVES AND SCOPE OF CONTROL
On 11 December 2007, the European Commission (EC) approved the Operational Programme for Cross-Border Cooperation Czech Republic – Republic of Poland 2007–2013 (OPPS ČR–POL).
€258,187,467 was earmarked for the implementation of the operational programme.
The total included a contribution from European Funds (ERDF) totalling €219,459,344 (85%) which must be used by the end of 2015.
The financial data on the absorption of EU funds from the Operational Programme Cross-border Cooperation Czech Republic – Republic of Poland 2007-2013 at the end of 2013 are presented in the following table:
The agreement on the cooperation of supreme audit institutions of the Czech Republic (SAO) and the Republic of Poland (NIK) was concluded by the presidents of both authorities on 27 February 2013 after previous mutual negotiations at the meeting of the heads of supreme audit institutions of the Visegrad Four, Austria and Slovenia, which took place in September 2012 in Lovasberény, Hungary.
The aim of the cooperation was to verify that the implementation of the operational programme is carried out in accordance with the legislation and with the binding programming documents or agreements, that the management and control system functions reliably, that the objectives of the operational programme are achieved and that the selected projects are implemented in accordance with the established conditions of the support provided.
This cooperation took the form of a parallel audit, in which the audit authorities independently examined, on the basis of their statutory mandate, subjects whose content had been discussed jointly beforehand.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
The audit carried out also included elements of a performance audit.
The selected topics were tested on a sample of projects at project partners on both sides of the border.
As a result of the checks carried out, the audit conclusions were published in the usual way by both authorities.
On the basis of the two audit conclusions set out in the Annex, this Joint Report was established.
The selected joint control sample included six projects (Annex 1) under aid area 1.1 — Strengthening transport accessibility and aid area 2.2 — Promoting tourism development.
Before the start of the audit, the drawing of funds from these two areas of support represented more than 60% of the funds paid to the beneficiaries by the end of 2012 from the ERDF for all implemented projects within the whole OPPS CR-POL.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
In addition to the auditing of a joint sample of six projects, the SAO audited another 13 projects and the NIK audited two others (all from the support area 1.1 and 2.2).
The funds paid to the beneficiaries from the ERDF, which were audited by the SAO and NIK, totalled 25,362,174 € (SAO) and 8,450,784 € (NIK).
The audited volume accounted for 41 % of the total amount paid to beneficiaries from aid areas 1.1 and 2.2.
MAIN CONTROL CONCLUSIONS
Both audits carried out independently by the SAO on the Czech side and NIK on the Polish side showed that the level of identified irregularities did not exceed the level of significance.
Implementation of the OPPS CR–POL, including the operation of the management and control system and implementation of selected projects, was successful.
The auditees, which were part of the management and control system, took steps to reduce the risk of misuse of funds and non-use of ERDF funds.
Proof of the effectiveness of the implemented projects under the operational programme is the high level of achievement of the set objectives, which was supported by the results of the survey of the social evaluation of the implementation of the projects.
SUMMARY OF CONTROL RESULTS
A. Evaluation of the management and control system
The coordinated approach made it possible to check the management and control system of the OPPS CR-POL implemented by the following institutions in both countries: the managing authority (Ministry of Regional Development of the Czech Republic), the national coordinator (Ministry of Regional Development of the Czech Republic) and the controllers (Centre for Regional Development and Voivodship).
At the same time, the coordinated audit allowed an evaluation of the tasks and functions carried out by the Joint Technical Secretariat and the Monitoring Committee.
During the evaluation of the management system, the audit focused on activities carried out in connection with the setting up and performance of the functions of the managing authority and the national coordinator, in particular in connection with the setting of the objectives of the programme and the monitoring of the progress achieved, evaluation activities and annual reports, the assessment, evaluation, selection and approval of project applications, the drawdown of programme funds and the fulfilment of the obligation to publish correct, complete and up-to-date information on grants, the control activities of the managing authority, the setting of the performance of activities and the results of the audits of the delegated body and the system of reporting and monitoring of irregularities and its compliance.
Furthermore, the work of the competent authorities in the area of risk analysis and the drawing up of control plans at the site of project implementation was examined and the evaluations of project applications carried out by independent evaluators were assessed.
The examination of the control system included a review of the administrative and on-the-spot checks that allowed for the approval of e.g. deliveries of goods and co-financed services, the eligibility of expenditure submitted for reimbursement, as well as the compliance of the disbursement of these funds and the related activities (or parts thereof) with national and EU legislation.
Significant deficiencies were found by the SAO audit in the area of publishing correct, complete and up-to-date information about the grants provided.
In the area of project evaluation and approval, a risk of non-transparency of this process has been identified.
Further shortcomings were identified in the setting of monitoring indicators and in the setting of the irregularity reporting system.
Based on these findings, the Supreme Audit Office assessed the control and management system implemented by the managing authority as partially effective.
The irregularities identified by NIK relating to the functioning of the management and control system in Poland in relation to violations of the applicable laws and rules were not assessed as significant.
These irregularities had an impact only on individual projects and did not have a significant impact on the implementation of the entire OPPS CR-POL.
Despite the identified discrepancies, NIK assessed the functioning of the system positively.
B. Evaluation of project results
The audit did not reveal any discrepancy between the indicators and targets set for each project and those defined by the operational programme.
In all audited projects, the expected results were achieved, the funded activities were implemented and the indicators (measurable parameters) set in the project applications of the operational programme were met.
The audit conclusions are in line with the result of the survey of the societal evaluation of project implementation described below.
An important aspect that the SAO and NIK addressed in their conclusions is the limited possibility of objective evaluation of the performance of the projected results of individual projects with regard to the objectives of individual projects as well as the objectives of the entire operational programme.
On initiative A public opinion survey was conducted by the Supreme Audit Chamber and was carried out in the summer of 2013 at the place of implementation of the projects selected in the joint control sample.
The survey and its evaluation were carried out by the School of Higher Vocational Education in Nysa (Poland).
In agreement with the Supreme Audit Office, the survey was conducted on both sides of the border between 2 820 respondents, both for the public in Poland in relation to the part of the project implemented by Polish project partners and for the Czech public in relation to the part of the project implemented in the Czech Republic.
The survey covered in particular the appropriateness, quality, information exchange, use of funds and the social function of the projects, including the investments made.
The number of respondents for each project is given in Annex 2.
The assessment of the survey responses indicated that the overall societal rating of the projects was good at 4.8 points (on a scale with a maximum of 8 points), which means that the results of the projects were viewed positively by the majority of respondents both in terms of the objectives and the other parameters set out in the project applications.
According to the respondents, the projects examined fulfilled the criteria expressed in the operational programme.
Respondents also noted the positive impact of investments on improving the quality of transport and tourism infrastructure, transport accessibility, tourist attractiveness, business conditions, quality of life of citizens and the renewal of contacts between residents of the border region.
On the other hand, the overall assessment of the responses indicated a certain difference between the actual level of achievement of the objectives/parameters (actual impact rate) and the level expected by the respondents.
This difference relates to the evaluation of the results of individual projects, where in some cases there is a significant difference.
The results of the survey were in line with the views of the participants in the panel discussions (target groups) carried out in the framework of the NIK audit on the Polish side, who pointed to the need to implement the projects and confirmed their positive public acceptance and the fulfilment of the expected results.
C. Evaluation of legality of audited projects
Beneficiaries of the aid were checked for compliance of the material and temporal implementation of the project with the timetable and content of the project application, fulfilment of the stated objectives and purpose of the project, fulfilment of binding monitoring indicators, management of changes in projects, handling of potential project revenues, fulfilment of the obligation to keep separate accounting records of project transactions, compliance with the prohibition of financing projects from other EU sources, implementation of the project partnership and compliance with its conditions, fulfilment of the obligatory publicity concerning in particular the dissemination of knowledge of sources of financing projects from the EU, compliance with the conditions of eligibility of project expenses, selection of contractors and verification of contracted services.
The total financial value of the deficiencies classified by the SAO and NIK as ineligible expenditure from EU funds was found to amount to € 85,094.
However, this amount did not exceed the materiality level established for the total volume of funds audited for the sample of projects.
Despite the detected irregularities, the SAO and NIK evaluated the implementation of the selected projects positively.
In most cases, the commitments under the terms of the aid were duly implemented by the beneficiaries.
The funds earmarked for the implementation of the audited projects were spent in accordance with EU regulations and national law.
However, seven out of the eight beneficiaries audited by NIK were found to have irregularities in the implementation of their material and financial obligations.
These were individual cases of a non-systemic nature, involving particularly inconsistent preparation of project documentation and breaches of public procurement rules, but which had no impact on the choice of contractor or the delivery of contractually agreed works.
Only in one of all projects audited by NIK can an irregularity detected relating to non-compliance with reporting obligations be classified as ineligible expenditure, amounting to PLN 43 003 (of which EUR 8 231 financed by the ERDF).
On the other side of the border, the SAO identified ineligible expenditure amounting to CZK 2,098,053 in three of the 19 projects audited (of which EUR 76,863 were financed by the ERDF).
Irregularities included failure to deliver the materials and works paid or expenditure for the modernisation of a property owned by a person who was not linked to the project either as a beneficiary of the aid or as a project partner.
RECOMMENDATIONS
The joint recommendation of the SAO and NIK concerns the improvement of the monitoring system, especially with regard to the consistent setting of proper monitoring indicators for all specific objectives of the priority axis of the operational programme.
In general, efforts should also be made to establish such indicators at programme and project level that allow a more reliable verification of compliance with the identified impacts.
Comments and recommendations resulting from inspections carried out on both sides of the border are included in separate audit conclusions of the SAO and NIK on CD.
COMMUNICATIONS
The report was drawn up in Czech, English and Polish and signed by the presidents of the supreme audit institutions of the Czech Republic and the Republic of Poland.
Public works contracts and prevention of corruption
Joint report on the parallel audit performed by the Czech Supreme Audit Office (SAO) and the German Federal Court of Auditors (BRH)
Introduction and Overview
Public procurement of goods and services by contracting authorities is very important for both the state and the private sector.
The role of the State is to use public resources in the most efficient and economical way and to ensure fair and regulated free competition.
In the European Union (EU), public procurement is a key factor for the success of the Single Economic Area.
The EU has set thresholds above which the contracting authority must meet certain requirements in order to guarantee a broad and unrestricted competitive environment across the EU.
EU Member States must therefore transpose European procurement law into their national legislation and put it into practice.
The Czech Republic and Germany have already done so.
For public contracts below the EU thresholds, the regulation of their award remains a national competence.
Moreover, public procurement is prone to corruption with a consequent high potential for harm.
Therefore, contracting entities are obliged to take reasonable measures in their procurement procedures to prevent corruption and unfair favouritism (prevention of corruption).
In January 2011, the Supreme Audit Office of the Czech Republic (Czech SAI – SAO) and the Federal Court of Auditors of Germany (German SAI – BRH) agreed to perform parallel audits in the area of procurement procedures and prevention of corruption in land and transport buildings.
Cooperation has also focused on the area of European procurement law as transposed into national legislation.
Controls included procurement below European thresholds in order to sanction the area of corruption prevention.
The audit findings of the two supreme audit institutions are summarised in a joint report, which is, among other things, addressed to international organisations such as the International Organisation of Supreme Audit Institutions (INTOSAI).
Areas of control and institutions audited
The BRH assessment is based on the sweeping inspections of the above-mentioned areas carried out by the German building authorities responsible for federal transport and land construction between 2011 and 2012.
For example, highways and large-scale civil engineering projects were evaluated.
The BRH included findings from past inspections in the evaluation.
The SAO's evaluation is based on audits carried out during the years 2008 to 2012, which focused on construction of roads, railway lines, water projects, large buildings (hospitals, cultural facilities, universities) and some other buildings in the Czech Republic.
In Germany, federal public procurement is mostly carried out by provincial building authorities.
In the Czech Republic, public procurement in the area of transport infrastructure is the responsibility of the state institutions for road and railway construction.
In the field of building construction, public procurement is carried out by entities receiving budget funds.
In both countries, the services of external experts are also used at the various stages of the procurement procedure.
The evaluation should take into account differences in public procurement legislation and the specificities of the construction market in both countries.
Structure of the Public Procurement Act in both countries
In Germany, the conduct of European procurement procedures is governed by laws and sub-statutory standards.
Procurement procedures limited to Germany shall be governed by budgetary law and the relevant administrative acts.
The internal administrative rules contain numerous additional provisions for both European and national procurement procedures.
Further details on the requirements of European procurement law and the prevention of corruption are enshrined in the so-called procurement manuals of the federal government, in which the requirements are elaborated or formulated in greater detail with regard to legal precedents in the area of procurement law.
In the Czech Republic, public procurement is governed by the Public Procurement Act and other legislation relating to contracts with a value above and below European thresholds.
The procurement law underwent substantial changes in 2012.
The new rules reinforced the transparency of procurement procedures, opened the way for more competition and reduced opportunities for corruption during procurement procedures.
Basic aspects of procurement law in both countries
As far as European procurement procedures are concerned, open and restricted procedures in particular need to be applied in both countries.
In Germany, contracts below the European threshold are awarded in accordance with national rules (public call for competition, restricted call for tenders, negotiated procedures), but these are practically identical to the above conditions for European procurement procedures.
Public procurement in Germany is characterised by the principle of preference for open procedures (where candidates are invited on a European scale) and public calls for participation (only in the case of national procedures).
In open procedures and invitations to tender, any candidate may submit a tender.
Another important feature of German procurement law is support for small and medium-sized companies.
This is achieved mainly by dividing the total contract into commercially specific lots and by dividing it into sub-contracts.
By awarding contracts divided into these lots, the largest possible number of candidates is given the chance to participate in the procedure.
The two characteristics of German procurement law are time-tested tools for obtaining good value for money.
There are more than 330,000 construction companies with 1.9 million employees registered in Germany.
In the Czech Republic, the conditions of European procedures must be applied also in cases of public procurement with a value below the EU limit.
An exception is the simplified below-threshold procedure, where the contracting authority may use less demanding procedures.
In accordance with European law, Czech procurement law includes the conditions under which the contract must be awarded in order to ensure competition and achieve a favourable price.
Current Czech legislation allows contracting authorities to choose between an open procedure and a restricted procedure.
There are 85,000 construction companies registered in the Czech Republic, of which about 1,900 have 20 or more employees.
Total volume of contracting authorities' public works contracts and proportion of individual procedures in both countries
In 2011, the total volume of construction works in Germany amounted to EUR 7 675 billion.
CZK 1,138 billion (15 %) of which was accounted for by the share of public contracts which, in addition to those awarded by the federal government, included contracts awarded by individual Länder and municipalities.
The total volume of projects of federal road and land constructions (without the construction of railways and water constructions) amounted to CZK 212.5 billion.
In 2011, the composition of federal works and transport contracts was as follows: 80,7 % open procedures and public calls; 12,7 % restricted management and calls;
6.6% negotiated procedures and direct award of contracts; 33.2% is the share of European procurement procedures.
The volume of public construction contracts in the Czech Republic reached 118.6 billion CZK in 2011, which was about 25% of the total volume of all construction works.
In 2009, the value reached CZK 194.0 billion, which was 37% of the total.
Structure of tender types in 2011: 65,1 % open procedures; 14,3 % restricted procedures; 9.5% negotiated procedures; 8.4% simplified procedures for low-threshold contracts; 2.7% other proceedings.
These figures include all public supply, service and works contracts awarded by contracting authorities in 2011.
These figures show that: parallel controls in both countries were targeted at a substantial part of public procurement procedures; The prevention of corruption, which must always be taken into account in procurement procedures, has a significant financial impact; it is of open importance. Procurement procedures are of key importance.
Summary of main results
The SAO and BRH carried out parallel audits of European works contracts with an emphasis on the prevention of corruption in their respective countries.
The audits focused on comparing legislation in both countries and the EU and on the implementation of public contracts in the field of land and transport construction.
The following are the key findings:
The EU Public Procurement Directive has been transposed into national law in both countries (points 1 and 3.1).
Preventing corruption is not only the aim of the EU directive, but also of the national procurement rules of the two countries, which differ partly in focus and detail.
In both countries, the essential requirement to be fulfilled by the tender documents is to include all the information needed for the preparation of the tenders.
The specification shall be neutral and shall not contain any requirements restricting competition.
The procurement documents in Germany generally meet these requirements.
The problem in the Czech Republic is the lack of definition of the subject of the contract.
In Germany, the division of contracts into lots is required where possible in order to promote a competitive environment.
According to BRH’s findings, this requirement is respected in Germany.
The SAO audits showed that in the Czech Republic, very large public works in the field of road construction are awarded without being divided into parts or are divided into very significant parts. This may distort competition and lead to restrictions on only a few large companies.
Such limitations of market potential may result in significant cost increases and risk unreliable price negotiations and impermissible agreements.
The legislation in both countries allows contracting authorities, under certain conditions, to subcontract additional works if the need arises after the conclusion of the contract (point 4.1).
In both countries, the requirements to correctly determine the type of procurement procedure are met.
In the Czech Republic, contracting entities are free to determine one of the open procurement procedures, namely the open procedure or the restricted procedure, in accordance with the applicable regulations.
German procurement and budget law rules enshrine the primacy of open procedures for both European tenders and national tenders, known as public calls for competition.
Open tendering procedures in both countries allow more bidders to submit a bid and also reduce the risk of corruption by making it more difficult for bidders to collude illegally.
At the same time, the best results in terms of value for money are achieved.
In the case of large contracts where the estimated total price reaches or exceeds the EU thresholds, the audited contracting entities have always launched a European procurement procedure.
The correct determination of the estimated value of the contract plays a very important role in the determination of the type of procurement procedure.
In Germany, databases are used for objective pricing of public works in which values are based on completed construction projects.
This is a sufficiently objective instrument.
In the Czech Republic, databases are used only in the field of road construction but are not yet an effective tool (point 4.2).
When awarding a works contract under European procurement procedures, it is necessary to publish prior information and a contract notice in order to strengthen competition by allowing candidates to prepare themselves in time for the submission of tenders.
As a rule, the construction authorities audited complied with this requirement.
In isolated cases, errors in the information released, incorrect application of provisions and/or a lack of controls may have impaired competition (item 4.3).
There is a greater risk of manipulation when selecting candidates to take part in a restricted procurement procedure.
Under EU law, the number of candidates invited must be high enough to guarantee genuine competition.
The selection criteria shall be objective, non-discriminatory and published in good time.
Beyond EU requirements, contracting entities in both countries should, as far as possible, rotate candidates who are invited to participate in the procedure when selecting suppliers.
In Germany, there are partly very detailed requirements to prevent risks of manipulation.
In some cases, the audited contracting entities did not comply with these requirements.
In the Czech Republic, selection decisions were not always credible (point 4.4).
In both countries, contracting authorities must ensure during the procurement procedure that all candidates wishing to submit a tender receive the same information.
Any agreements between those candidates and between them and third parties involved in the preparation of the specifications must be avoided as far as possible in order to avoid restricting competition by means of illegal agreements.
In Germany, the measures are more thorough than in the Czech Republic.
However, the German contracting authorities audited did not meet this requirement in several cases (point 4.5).
In both countries, the principle of cross-checking and transparency must be applied when opening envelopes.
The requirements for labelling, documentation, protection and confidentiality of tenders are intended to limit the scope for manipulation and are particularly detailed in Germany.
Checks in both countries found only a few cases of irregularities (point 4.6).
The evaluation and evaluation of tenders is regulated procedurally in both countries.
The relevant German measures are more comprehensive but have not always been respected by the audited contracting authorities.
For example, contracting entities transmitted their tenders to third parties for consultation without prior assessment, without properly documenting any suspicious circumstances.
The tenders were thus inadequately protected against subsequent manipulation (paragraph 4.7).
For reasons of legal protection, tenderers must be informed about the intended contract award before the latter is finalised.
This legal protection rule is also designed to prevent corruptive action.
The law of the Czech Republic provides for judicial review of contract award procedures also for national tenders.
Only in few cases, the notification of the enterprises was not appropriately ensured (item 4.8).
The procurement documents for the audited contracting entities in both countries were not always complete or fully credible.
In the case of several procurement procedures, the transparency requirements applied at European level were not always adequately respected in the minutes.
Moreover, some of the weaknesses identified reduced the preventive anti-corruption function of the dossier.
Contracting entities in both countries mostly fulfilled their reporting obligations (paragraph 4.9).
Summary of the main principles of European procurement law and prevention of corruption
European procurement law
In order to ensure free competition in the EU, the European Parliament and the Council of the European Union adopted, inter alia, a Directive on the coordination of procedures for the award of public works contracts, public supply contracts and public service contracts to ensure the free movement of goods and services and a Directive on the coordination of laws, regulations and administrative provisions relating to the application of review procedures to the award of public supply contracts and public works contracts (the Remedies Directive), both of which were to be transposed into national law.
The EU's general procurement principles include in particular: the requirement for a competitive environment; the requirement for equal treatment or non-discrimination; the requirement for transparency.
EU procurement principles include The Directive on the coordination of public procurement procedures also includes provisions for construction projects with a total estimated value of € 5 million or more.
These provisions shall affect: the content of the procurement documents; the type of procurement procedure used; the publication of certain information before, during and after the procedure; compliance with the deadline for receipt of tenders; qualification requirements that tenderers may be required to meet; criteria for selecting the most suitable tender.
The legal protection of tenderers is governed by the Remedies Directive.
In the event of a potential breach of procurement law, tenderers may have the contracting authority's decision reviewed in accordance with the review procedures.
Both countries have transposed EU requirements into national legislation.
Preventing corruption
The importance of preventing corruption
There is no legal definition in either country of what corruption actually means.
In general, corruption is understood as an abuse of a personal (confidential) position for one's own benefit.
As a rule, this means that such abuse is instigated by those who benefit from it.
Corruption can be an obstacle to competition and can cause serious economic losses.
Overall corruption-related damages are estimated to amount to 5% of world economic output.
For the EU area, these costs are estimated at 1% of the EU's economic output.
Where a contracting authority is involved in a contract, agreements restricting competition or allowing dishonest conduct may undermine confidence in the integrity, ability and overall work of the public administration.
Preventing corruption is therefore an essential task for contracting authorities.
Practices and procedures must be monitored, particularly in areas particularly prone to corruption, so that adequate measures can be taken to prevent it.
Taking preventive measures and carrying out effective controls can substantially reduce the vulnerability of the decision-making process to corruption.
As a rule, the award of works contracts is considered to be an area particularly prone to corruption.
Measures to prevent corruption
In Germany, corruption is prosecutable under criminal and labour law, in particular active and passive bribery, receiving and offering significant benefits.
The German Federal Government has issued a manual of recommendations on the prevention of corruption in the federal administration, according to which the following principles are applied throughout the procurement process, outside the risk analysis: the priority of open procurement; the principle of multiple control and transparent decision-making; the separation of the planning, procurement and accounting phases; accurate and complete documentation of the procurement procedure.
In addition, other measures having a preventive effect against corruption have become part of the procurement law.
In 2011, the competent Federal Ministry of Information Technology introduced a system to monitor the procurement of federal roads.
Using this system, data such as estimated costs, total value of the contract and involvement of private design firms can be fully recorded and analysed in order to identify potential irregularities.
In the Czech Republic, corruption is prosecuted under criminal law, and the level of labour or civil law is also an option.
The legal requirement of transparency, equal treatment and non-discrimination throughout the procurement process (preparation and implementation) is the most important part of preventing corruption.
Anti-corruption measures in this area have been set out by the government in the "Government Anti-Corruption Strategy 2011-2012", which has been updated for 2013 and 2014.
Some of these measures have been incorporated into the Public Procurement Act and subsequent implementing regulations.
The new legislation includes the following basic provisions: lowering the thresholds for small-scale contracts; the obligation of prior notification by the contracting authority, including the justification for the contract; the prohibition on limiting the number of tenderers in restricted procedures and the abolition of the general possibility to limit the number of tenderers by drawing lots; obligation to publish contracts, including annexes; obligation to publish actual lists of subcontractors.
Results of checks relating to the subsequent stages of the procurement procedure
Procurement documents
The procurement documents shall contain all the information necessary to enable tenderers to prepare their tenders in an appropriate form.
In particular, the documentation shall make it possible to prepare the tender price.
In order to do so, tenderers must not be exposed to risks which they could not have anticipated.
The tender documents shall contain, in particular, the specifications, the requirements for proving the qualifications of the tenderers and the criteria for selecting the most advantageous tender.
European law requirements and prevention of corruption
In particular, European law contains requirements relating to technical specifications.
Specifications must be as restrictive of competition as possible and all interested parties must have equal access to them.
The specification shall be neutral.
Requirements for specific specific products are permitted in the tender documents on a very limited scale.
The basic principle of preventing corruption is that tender documents should not contain requirements that could restrict competition.
Furthermore, the documentation should avoid ambiguities or inaccurate data which create room for speculation and manipulation in the evaluation of tenders.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Same or similar provisions
Both countries have transposed EU legal provisions into their national legislation.
The basic principle is that specifications should be given in general terms without reference to a specific product or brand.
The specifications shall specify the deliveries and services required and detail the number of units of work and deliveries.
In normal cases, prices should be stated by units of works or supplies (e.g. in CZK/m or in CZK/item).
It is permissible to define the required works in terms of their functionality only in exceptional cases.
In principle, the rule is that the specifications must be drawn up completely and unambiguously.
They must contain precise quantities and must not include works and supplies the necessity of which is not obvious.
Reimbursement in the form of lump sums or time worked should be avoided wherever possible.
These parts of the procurement documents shall exclude speculation and manipulation based on ambiguous specifications.
The specifications must enable each tenderer to prepare its tender with certainty and without excessive preparatory work.
The invitation to tender shall be published only when the procurement documents are complete.
Different provisions
In the Czech Republic, there is no obligation for contracting authorities to award construction contracts in lots.
A contracting authority may divide a contract into several separate lots where the nature of the subject-matter of the contract so permits.
In Germany, the rule of dividing works into lots is required, either quantitatively (partial deliveries) or according to specific criteria (specialised lots).
This allows SMEs to compete, increases the number of potential bidders and makes price fixing more difficult.
This type of award is usually considered the most appropriate in terms of providing value for money.
In the Czech Republic, tender documents must contain evaluation criteria in national and European procurement procedures.
In doing so, the tenderer should be informed comprehensively and exhaustively of the criteria on the basis of which its tender will be evaluated.
In Germany, the requirement to include evaluation criteria in the notification applies only to European invitations to tender.
In national procurement procedures, it is necessary to include evaluation criteria only in the contract record, which is an internal administrative document.
If the tender documents are prepared by authorised independent experts, the German regulations require that any information which would help to identify the specific firms, architects or designers involved be excluded from the tender documents.
This is to prevent individual construction firms from obtaining any additional information or other undue competitive advantage without the contracting authority's knowledge through contacts with these independent experts.
This is also the reason why independent experts may not publish tender documents and make them available for screening.
The contracting authority is the single point of contact for construction companies.
The Czech Republic does not currently have a similar provision.
In the case of large construction projects, additional works or modifications of existing works are almost always necessary after the original contract is concluded.
The legislation of both countries allows the award of a multi-work contract within a certain limit to the same contractor without the need to publish and conduct a new procurement procedure.
The volume of additional works under both European and German legislation may not exceed 50 % of the original value of the contract.
In addition, the additional works must be necessary and specific to the main contract.
In the Czech Republic, a stricter limit of 20% is applied for the award of additional works.
This may entail the risk of a greater time lag in the execution of the contract and the possible inclusion of a new entity in an already running project.
If the contract for additional works is awarded to the supplier of the original contract, German law contains a provision that prevents suppliers from arbitrarily increasing the price of the modified works or additional works.
The calculations shall be made taking into account the prices agreed in the original contract.
In addition to extra work, less work may occur during construction, i.e. work that was not needed.
In the Czech Republic, less work and more work cannot be compensated for.
Main control findings
BHR
Technical specifications and product-neutral invitation to tender
In isolated cases, the specifications referred to national technical requirements or standards, but these have since been replaced by EU standards.
In some cases, the specifications included information requiring a particular brand name, e.g. for the resistance system of public signs, to prevent water ingress or for noise barriers, although this was not necessary.
In some cases, the specifications were so detailed that only one product met the requirements.
Quantity specifications broken down into items
The BRH did not, in a single case of control of procurement procedures, notice non-compliance with the general requirements for the invitation to tender for supplies and services under the quantitative specifications broken down into lots.
Completeness of specifications
In most of the audited cases, the tender documents were fully prepared before the announcement of the call for tenders.
In various cases, however, documents had to be modified during the procurement procedure when changes in the headings or timetable had to be taken into account.
In rare cases, the tender documents included works and supplies for which the need was uncertain.
One tender dossier for demolition work contained almost exclusively such items.
Division of contract and award criteria
In many cases, the required justification for not dividing deliveries into lots has not been demonstrated.
The BRH did not find any shortcomings in the criteria for evaluating tenders prior to the award of the contract.
Inclusion of the names of authorised independent experts
Many of the documents mentioned the authorised experts involved.
The names appeared on the opening pages of the documents or on the plans or attached opinions.
In one case, the contracting authority even invited tenderers to contact the explicitly appointed architect with their questions on the procurement procedure.
In the case of building projects, these cases were found mainly in the tender documents, where self-employed experts were entrusted with the elaboration of the specifications.
The contracting authorities indicated that they did not have sufficient staffing capacity to examine the tender documents in detail.
SAO
Technical specifications and product-neutral invitation to tender
In the case of a public contract for the construction of a house with 12 apartments, the reference was directly to specific manufacturers and product brands.
The contracting authority did not allow the use of other similar types of products, nor did it give reasons why it required the supply of specific products.
For the construction of university facilities, the tender documents included references to specific business names and specific product designations.
Overview of specifications and sub-specifications
For the construction of certain industrial zones, the contracting authority did not clearly define in the procurement documents the quantity and type of works, supplies or services required in the statement of assessment and defined only the enclosed space in cubic metres as the unit of measurement for each building.
Completeness of specifications
During the procurement procedure for the construction of the ring road, the tender documents for the restricted procedure were amended four times between August and September 2009.
This was due to changes in the project documentation caused by the extension of the scope of the contract and the change of technical conditions.
In the case of the construction of social buildings, the tender documentation had defects that forced the creation of additional works (lack of precise focus, lack of sufficient construction technical research, lack of drawings).
In the tender documents for the construction of the large library, the interior elements were mentioned only descriptively, without specifying qualitative requirements for the products.
Supply of interior equipment was provided by subcontractors, who purchased some above-standard and very expensive equipment.
Parts and criteria for the award of the contract
In the Czech Republic there is no obligation to award the contract in parts.
Analysis of three major road constructions during the construction of the road circuit showed that when they were awarded in an open procedure in parts (separately after selected buildings), it was possible to achieve savings of up to CZK 790 million from the achieved public procurement price of CZK 15,508 million.
For example, when awarding six buildings as a separate part of a public contract, savings of EUR 62.5 million could be achieved.
CZK, i.e. 25% of the contracted price and with the award of 20 bridges as a separate part of the public contract, a saving of CZK 287.8 million, i.e. also 25%, could be achieved.
When constructing roads, the contracting authority laid down in the procurement documents qualification criteria which restricted the participation of small suppliers in the procurement procedure.
In the tender conditions of the open tender for a contractor for one construction of a motorway, the contracting authority required, among other things, the contractor's practice of implementing at least three road constructions with a financial volume of more than CZK 700 million each, completed or put into operation during the last five years.
There are only nine companies in the Czech Republic that meet the condition of completion of buildings over CZK 700 million in the last five years.
The public contract for the supply of another motorway construction in the open procedure included six separate constructions for which the contracting authority requested a minimum annual volume of works in the last three years of EUR 12 billion.
Two thirds of financial volume of road construction works (i.e. € 2.5 billion out of € 3.7 billion) in the period of 2008–2009 were realized by 5 suppliers or individually either in form of consortium.
In 46 contracts for a total of CZK 13.2 billion, there were only two or even one bidder.
The SAO pointed out that limiting market potential can lead to a large increase in costs.
Multi-Work Area
In two cases, the contracting authority did not conclude a written contract for the supply of additional works during the construction of the ring road and did not award the additional works by any of the procedures specified in the Act.
The value of non-approved additional works in one case was CZK 1,591.4 million (increase of 21%) and in the other case CZK 4,135.8 million (increase of 91%).
The cause of the additional works was mainly the ambiguous definition of the quantity and type of work required.
In the case of the construction of university facilities, the contracting authority did not include in the tender documents certain works and supplies whose necessity for the completion and approval of the construction was known to it at the time of the award of the contract.
For these works and supplies he concluded amendments to the contract with the contractor of the work with an increase in the price, in a negotiated procedure without publication, even though the volume of additional works exceeded the national limit of 20% of the value of the original contract.
In connection with the construction of the Palace of Justice, the contracting authority reserved the right to award additional works without a procurement procedure.
During the construction, the contracting authority increased the scope of the contract by CZK 50.5 million.
The unit prices of the additional works, determined without competition, were significantly higher than comparable works carried out by the same supplier.
Other findings
The SAO found on a selected sample of buildings that the average construction cost of one kilometre of a four-lane motorway in the Czech Republic is CZK 355.0 million.
According to long-term data obtained from the German Ministry of Construction, the average cost in Germany per kilometre section of a four-lane motorway is CZK 260.0 million.
This comparison suggests that acquiring a motorway in the Czech Republic is more expensive than in Germany.
Summary
The legal provisions relating to the tender documents are almost identical in both countries and comply with the requirements of European law.
The aim is to ensure full and equal competition and the prevention of corruption.
The specifications must be sufficiently precise to allow interested parties to calculate the costs and prepare their bids.
Moreover, this requirement limits the scope for speculation and the contracting authority is protected against the need to award additional works.
In Germany, the basic legal provisions on procurement documents are complemented by additional rules to further reduce the possibilities for restricting competition and for carrying out manipulations.
Although there are no detailed requirements of this kind in the Czech Republic, greater transparency is achieved by publishing the award criteria.
Specification requirements are mostly met in both countries.
In some cases, however, shortcomings have been identified which could lead to a reduction of competition.
The current rule in Germany requiring the division of large construction projects into lots seems appropriate for strengthening the competitive environment and achieving cost-effective results.
The SAO found that in the Czech Republic, awarding contracts for large-scale projects without awarding them in parts or dividing them into large-scale projects can limit the number of candidates to a small number of large suppliers.
As a result of the strict requirement for bidder references applied in the Czech Republic for the selection of bidders for road construction works, public contracts are awarded only to a small number of bidders and small and medium-sized companies have no chance to compete.
The limited number of bidders also increases the risk of price fixation and other illegal arrangements.
Regarding the award and reimbursement of additional contracts for additional works or modified works, regulatory approaches differ between the two countries.
Where the value of the additional works under European and German law does not exceed 50 % of the value of the original contract, the additional works may be awarded without a call for tenders and a contract notice.
The Czech Republic has a stricter limit of 20%.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
This limit is often exceeded by contracting authorities.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
If the 20 % limit is exceeded, the additional works must be awarded on the basis of a new procurement procedure.
Dividing works into parts avoids restricting competition and consequent adverse consequences.
Determination of the type of procurement procedure with regard to EU limits
By specifying the type of procurement procedure, the contracting authority defines the range of potential suppliers that will be admitted to the tender.
The contracting authority may leave the range of potential suppliers unlimited or may limit the range according to the criteria specified.
A European procurement procedure must be conducted for large-scale design works with a value that reaches or exceeds the EU thresholds.
European law requirements and prevention of corruption
In choosing the type of procedure, the contracting authority must, in particular, comply with the requirements of European law relating to the competitive environment and transparency.
If the estimated total cost of the contract for the requested works reaches or exceeds the current EU limit of €5.0 million ($125 million), an open or restricted procurement procedure at European level is always required.
In exceptional cases, a negotiated procedure or a competitive dialogue is permitted.
If the construction project reaches the EU limit, the contracting authority may, to a limited extent, deviate from the European procurement procedure by issuing a national tender if the total value of the relevant part is below the threshold of 1.0 million € (25 million CZK) and the total value of all parts does not exceed 20% of the value of the whole contract estimate.
Dividing up a building project or deliberately estimating its cost too low in order to avoid a fundamental obligation to launch a European tender is not acceptable.
Choosing the type of procedure is very important for preventing corruption, as reducing competition substantially increases the risk of manipulation.
Therefore, a detailed justification of the chosen type of procedure, in particular in relation to the obligation to apply European procurement law, is an important part of the tender documentation.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Same or similar provisions
Both countries have fully implemented EU legal requirements on procurement procedures and limits in their national legislation.
Different provisions
The Czech Republic has introduced the principle of equal validity of open and restricted procedures also for procurement procedures at national level.
Open and restricted procedures can always be chosen, while other types of proceedings are only allowed if the conditions laid down by law are met.
Unlike the Czech legislation, the relevant German legislation on budgetary law and the principles of prevention of corruption prefers open procedures or a public invitation to participate in the procedure.
Other types of proceedings, including restricted proceedings, are permitted only in exceptional cases where legislation so permits.
Such exceptions must be justified and documented.
The principle of preference for an open procedure is intended to ensure that a limitation of competition is avoided as far as possible and that the opportunity for manipulation (pre-selected specific candidates) is limited.
In Germany, this principle of public procurement is central to preventing corruption.
A correct estimation of the expected contract price is an important basis for deciding on the appropriate type of procurement procedure.
In estimating the costs of building projects, contracting entities in Germany can rely on the budget and cost databases of public administrations and other organisations.
These have been compiled on the basis of an analysis of completed construction projects and include, inter alia, standard cost values for building types, building units, building components and partial works.
On the basis of such standard cost values it is possible to estimate the value of the contract, planned project or works.
Only a few building authorities in Germany already have a similar database for road construction.
However, the database is expected to be expanded at national level.
Contracting entities in the Czech Republic do not use any central database or average prices of comparable buildings to determine the expected costs of public contracts for ground construction.
In many cases, the determination of costs is based on an estimate of the entity financing the project or on the value of the resources available to that entity.
Measures for standardisation are still at an early stage.
Under the 2011-2012 Anti-Corruption Programme, the relevant ministries are to develop a database on the current prices of standardised commodities.
The application of a price system for the determination of the expected price of a contract is determined by a new regulation ordering the detailed definition of the subject-matter of the contract.
The pricing system should serve as a set of information on construction and assembly work, materials and products and other data in order to calculate the necessary costs and establish unit prices.
The Ministry of Transport has issued normative prices for transport constructions in order to check them, which are regularly updated.
The database is designed to determine the price of the project (investment) plan and should play a comparative function in the next stages of construction preparation.
Main control findings of BRH
Selection of procurement procedure
The audited contracting authorities generally awarded contracts by open procedure or by means of a public call for participation, thus complying with the requirements of budgetary and procurement legislation.
In 2011, the share of public tenders in the total volume of federal building projects in the field of building construction was 59.5%.
In road construction, this figure was 94.8%.
BRH found that in some cases the use of non-public tendering was not sufficiently justified.
In the case of the blanket check, BRH found, based on information collected from more than 16 000 procedures, that the restricted procedure has a clear disadvantage compared to the public procedure and the public call and that the restricted procedures also increase the risk of manipulation and corruption.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
According to the BRH study, tender prices in restricted and negotiated procedures were between 6 % and 10 % higher than in public tenders.
Parallel studies by the Ministry of Construction and the Regional Court of Auditors confirmed differences in value for money obtained in public and restricted procedures.
A study by the Ministry of Construction even found that prices in restricted procedures were twice as high as prices in public procedures.
Compliance with EU limits
For projects with an estimated total contract value of more than €5 million (CZK 125 million), audited contracting authorities usually prepared procurement plans indicating which works would be awarded in the European procurement procedure and which in the national procedure.
According to the procurement plans, the proportion of works awarded under European rules has always exceeded 80 % of the total works.
There was no case of circumvention of the requirement for a European procedure in the form of an excessively low cost estimate.
However, in some cases the minimum proportion for pan-European management was not reached due to a calculation error.
In the course of carrying out the works, some contracting authorities have unlawfully changed the procurement plan.
They did not provide any justification for these changes and did not demonstrate that the minimum proportion of European governance would subsequently be respected.
As a rule, the proportion of European procedures by project accounts was lower than that laid down in the procurement plan.
If the expected values of the construction project contracts exceeded the European limits, the contracting authorities did not apply the European procedure to all individual parts with a value exceeding €1 million ($25 million).
Instead, they awarded the relevant contracts on the basis of a national procedure without giving any valid reasons for the need for a derogation from the requirement.
SAO
Choice of procurement procedure type
In the case of several major construction projects (e.g. construction of a road section, facilities for a major sporting event and construction of apartments), the contracting authority divided one project into several parts, although these sub-contracts were of the same or similar nature.
The split-up reduced the expected value of the contract and the contracts were awarded under a less demanding regime or even outside the statutory regime.
Methods for calculating the estimated price
Price normatives applicable to road construction projects have not been an effective tool for setting the expected price.
They were based on prices quoted in the finalised procedures, which were however subject to both inefficiencies and excessive profit for suppliers.
The SAO found that actual prices for three parts of the road circuit were approximately 37% lower than these price norms.
The Ministry of Transport is to update the standards not only in relation to prices from the past, but also in relation to other comparable markets and price developments.
For land-based buildings, the estimated price was based on the project budget carried out by the architects or on an estimate by the investor.
The actual price then differed significantly from the estimated price.
The estimated cost of building a large library was twice as high as the contract price, but during the implementation it increased by CZK 54 million, i.e. by 27%.
Similarly, for the construction of the depository of this library, the contract price was 33% lower than the expected price.
In other cases, the price of the successful applicant was higher than the expected price and increased further during the implementation.
In the case of the construction of a large library, this was by CZK 400 million, i.e. by 22%. In the case of the construction of the Palace of Justice, the actual price increased disproportionately during the construction (by 139 %) compared to the increase in functional areas (by only 12 %).
Summary
Both countries have implemented European provisions on the choice of the type of procurement procedure and on compliance with European limits in their national legislation, so that the respective national regulations of both countries are almost identical.
However, the principle of public procedures in Germany is stricter than the European regulation and the current national regulation in the Czech Republic.
Compared to a narrower procurement procedure, a public procurement procedure or a call for tenders gives the possibility to a larger number of competitors to participate in the procurement procedure.
These types of procurement procedures generate a large number of bids that guarantee the best value for money effect.
Corruption prevention is significantly reinforced as the pool of candidates is not limited by decisions taken in advance by the contracting authority, which gives room for manipulation and fixing of prices between tenderers.
The studies confirmed the advantages of the German provision on the preference of open procedures or public calls for participation.
Czech law allows contracting authorities to award contracts in open or restricted procedures.
However, the latest legislation of the Public Procurement Act introduces a prohibition for contracting authorities to limit the number of tenderers in restricted procedures.
The restricted procedure has now come very close to an open procedure.
In Germany, the requirement of preference for an open procedure or a public call for competition is largely fulfilled by the contracting authority, while in the Czech Republic some projects were awarded in violation of the law in order to circumvent the requirement to choose the appropriate type of procurement procedure.
However, in the case of some projects carried out in Germany, European limits were not respected, resulting in a lack of a minimum share in pan-European procedures.
In the Czech Republic, a satisfactory system facilitating the correct calculation of the expected costs of construction works and projects has not yet been implemented.
Where appropriate, contracting entities should ensure, through more extensive controls, that the requirements for the selection of the type of procurement procedure and the volume of contracts awarded in European procedures are fulfilled or should create the conditions necessary to allow the correct calculation of expected costs.
Preliminary information and notice of initiation
In cases where contracts are awarded in the framework of a European procedure, the contracting authority must publish a procurement report.
Preliminary information and tender notices are an important basis for European competition.
European law requirements and prevention of corruption
By means of prior notices, the contracting authority must indicate the essential specifications of the envisaged projects.
Prior notifications are required for projects for which the total cost estimate is equal to or greater than the European limit and when the contracting authority wishes to reduce the time limit for the submission of tenders in individual procurement procedures.
The contracting authority is obliged to publish a contract notice with detailed information for all public contracts requiring a European procedure.
The information shall be binding on the contracting authority after publication.
