The notice shall contain, inter alia, the specifications, the time limits for the procedure and for the completion of the contract, the specific requirements and selection criteria and their weightings.
The Common Procurement Vocabulary shall be used to describe the subject of the contract.
Preliminary information and contract notices are published in the EU in the Official Journal of the EU and on the profile of the contracting authority.
In order to avoid discrimination against foreign candidates, notices at national level should not contain any additional information and should not be published before the text of the notice is sent to the Publications Office of the EU.
Incomplete or inaccurate information in the advance notice may distort competition or even facilitate manipulation.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Same or similar provisions
Both countries have fully implemented the EU provisions on prior information and procurement notices in their national legislation.
Different provisions
In the Czech Republic, preliminary information and notices must also be published for contracts for which a European procedure is not required.
The procurement procedure may not start earlier than one month after the dispatch of the prior information notice.
Provisional disclosure may be waived only in exceptional cases.
In Germany, prior information is not required in cases where a European procedure is not necessary.
In the case of national proceedings, the public notice must be published on the federal government website and, where appropriate, in the press.
In the case of restricted procedures without competition between participants, the notice must be published if the estimated contract price is equal to or higher than €25,000 ($625,000).
Advance notifications must explain all the planned parts of the project.
This is an important requirement given the number of small orders in Germany.
The division of projects into smaller parts is less frequent in the Czech Republic and this measure is considered less important.
Main control findings
BRH
Prior notice
The contracting authorities published a prior notice of projects which included works to be awarded in the European procedure.
In some open procedures, they shortened the deadline, although the number of days between the sending of the pre-notification and the sending of the contract notice was less than 52 days.
In the case of other abridged procedures, the annual time limits for prior information had expired and should have been reinstated.
In many cases, the prior notification did not indicate the anticipated division of the works into several parts.
Procurement notice
Some notifications contained codes from the Common Vocabulary which deviated from the content of the pre-notification or did not specify precisely the work to be carried out.
In rare cases, notifications at national level included more information, e.g. on deadlines, or were published before the notice was published in the Official Journal of the EU.
Ex-ante transparency
One contracting authority hired an external company to publish a national notice.
It published a notice on its platform, the use of which was subject to a fee.
However, the contracting authority did not check whether the company had to comply with the requirement to forward the notice to a free online government portal.
The sample showed that only a third of the notifications were actually published on the government portal.
SAO
Procurement notice
In the case of the construction of a waste water treatment plant, the contracting authority did not set out in the tender notice for the contractor the criteria according to which it would reduce a large number of tenders for the evaluation procedure.
The manner in which the tenders were selected (reduced) for evaluation was not substantiated by the contracting authority.
Reducing the number of bids was allowed by previous legislation.
For the construction of a ring road, the contracting authority did not define, in the notice of initiation of the restricted procedure or in the qualification documents, the values by which it would assess the extent to which the qualification criteria were met or the number of candidates to be invited.
The contracting authority assessed the fulfilment of the qualification as having been fulfilled by all six candidates in the restricted procedure, but excluded one of them on the grounds that he had finished in the last sixth place.
The procedure of the contracting authority was not transparent as it did not publish in advance information on what qualification data would be evaluated and how.
Publication of calls
In the case of building modifications in former military sites, the contracting authority unlawfully failed to publish a written invitation to tender.
Conclusions and recommendations/summary
The two-stage information process with prior information and the publication of a notice of initiation is an important tool for meeting the requirements of transparency and strengthening the competitive environment in European procedures.
By extending the obligation to publish prior information in the Czech Republic also for national procurement procedures and reinforcing this obligation in Germany for restricted procedures, both countries have increased the transparency of procurement procedures and improved the competitive environment.
Selection of candidates in restricted procurement procedures
Contrary to the open procedure, limited procurement procedures do not allow the participation of all potential candidates who would be interested.
In restricted procedures, the contracting authority shall first select the potential suppliers that will be asked to submit a tender.
European law requirements and prevention of corruption
EU provisions allow for a limit on the number of applicants in European restricted procedures.
However, the restricted procedure requires at least five tenderers to be invited to submit a tender and, in the case of a negotiated procedure with publication, at least three potential tenderers must be invited.
In any event, the number of candidates invited must be sufficiently large to ensure genuine competition.
The selection criteria must be objective and non-discriminatory and published in advance.
The selection of potential candidates to be invited is highly susceptible to manipulation, e.g. the preferences of some of them.
Reducing competition to a few bidders can make it easier for them to collude.
This has a negative impact on the outcome of the proceedings.
Therefore, the contracting authority must take measures to prevent manipulation and ensure that it has effective control in order to ensure that the procedure is transparent and credible.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Same or similar provisions
The provisions for the selection of candidates are similar in both countries.
In addition to the requirements of the European Directive, contracting entities should reward the tenderers they invite to submit a tender.
Nor is it permissible to restrict competition from a regional point of view.
Contracting entities shall document and justify the selection of tenderers and the selection process.
Different provisions
In Germany it is required to invite at least three candidates in the case of a restricted procedure and several suitable candidates in the case of a negotiated procedure.
Except in two cases, the new Czech regulations do not allow to limit the number of candidates approached in the case of restricted and negotiated procedures.
In Germany, the ‘four eyes’ principle must be respected when selecting candidates.
While one worker proposes a list, another must substantially modify the proposal, e.g. by removing a proposed candidate or appointing another.
This is intended to avoid a situation where an individual can definitively identify the pool of candidates who will take part in the procedure.
In the absence of such a provision, far-reaching illegal collusion between participants is allowed.
The list of tenderers to be invited to submit a tender shall be confidential and shall not be divulged.
Authorised independent experts are also obliged to do so.
There is no similar measure or measure for the confidential treatment of the list of candidates in the Czech Republic.
Under the German provisions, they may not nominate external companies but may submit proposals to the contracting authority.
There is no such requirement in the Czech Republic.
Main control findings
BRH
Number of Leads
Except in a few cases, the requirement to reach a minimum number of candidates was met.
An analysis of almost 7 000 works contracts with a value below the national threshold showed that, in the case of restricted procedures, contracting authorities invited an average of 6,6 potential tenderers and, in the case of direct negotiated procedures, an average of 4,4 tenderers.
Change of suppliers and regional restrictions
In particular, in the area of building construction, the contracting authorities did not change the range of applicants interviewed, as they had good experience with those with whom they had previously cooperated.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
The contracting authorities argued that, in the case of a non-public procedure, they should ensure that they reach out to tenderers whose suitability they have already ascertained beforehand.
Although there was no evidence of unlawful regional restrictions, the pool of bidders in the non-public procedure included a larger proportion of regional bidders than was the case in the public procedures.
The contracting authorities explained this by the low level of the contract compared to the public procurement procedure and the high cost of travel for potential tenderers.
Cross-checking principle and proposals of self-employed professionals
The contracting authorities largely ignored the obligation to amend the list of candidates proposed by the self-employed professionals.
They argued in particular that there was a lack of staff, time pressure or inadequate knowledge of a suitable candidate.
In many cases, the records did not show who had proposed the candidate who had been asked to participate in the procedure.
An examination of some samples showed that the list of potential candidates was drawn up by self-employed professionals and accepted by the contracting authority without changes.
As a result, contracting authorities did not adequately protect themselves from potential manipulation.
Confidentiality of the treatment of lists of potential candidates
One contracting authority kept a list of candidates for invitations to tender in a database to which all employees had access at any time.
This allowed manipulation or unauthorised disclosure of the names of presumed or admitted bidders, who could then agree on the price.
Documentation
The dossier gave reasons for the selection of candidates only in a few cases.
SAO
Criteria for the selection of candidates
During the construction of the road circuit, the applicants did not have information about what data about their qualifications would be evaluated and how.
For the action related to the development and modernisation of waterways and ports, the tender notice for participation in the restricted procedure did not set out criteria for limiting the number of tenderers.
All eight candidates fulfilled the qualifications, but this exceeded the number to which the contracting authority restricted participation in the procurement procedure.
The Commission then selected the candidates by random drawing of lots, which was legal at the time.
That procedure should already have been mentioned as a criterion in the contract notice.
Summary
In view of the high risk of manipulation in the selection of the winning bidder in restricted procurement procedures, both countries have put in place special measures to prevent undue restrictions of competition.
The German rules limit such opportunities for manipulation by means of so-called cross-checking (the four-eyes principle), by maintaining the confidentiality of the list of candidates and by prohibiting external companies from making proposals for the selection of candidates.
The Czech rules aim to prevent potential restrictions with a view to manipulation, in particular by an unlimited number of bidders.
However, the audit found that some of the above rules were not sufficiently applied and that an inadmissible restriction of the pool of candidates was not prevented in some cases.
Contracting entities are obliged to completely eliminate these shortcomings.
Submission of tenders
The stage of the procurement procedure where potential tenderers can draw up tenders on the basis of the tender documents they have received starts with the publication of the tender information and/or the invitation to tenderers.
Until the date on which the envelopes are opened, potential tenderers may submit their tenders to the contracting authority in order to compete for the award of the contract.
European law requirements and prevention of corruption
EU law does not prescribe specific requirements at this stage of the procurement procedure.
The contracting entity shall follow the national rules governing this stage of the procedure and avoid potential manipulation.
Those provisions relate in particular to organisation and control.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Same or similar provisions
The principle is that tender documents on the basis of which potential suppliers prepare their bids must always be sent by the contracting authority.
Design offices with a management interest should not fulfil this role.
The contracting authority must ensure that all candidates have received the same information during this stage of the procurement procedure.
In the event of confusion in the procurement documents or where further questions arise, only the contracting authority may supply information to tenderers.
The design offices responsible for drawing up the tender documents may not provide any information.
During this entire phase, any contact between the design office and the tenderers must be avoided.
They must remain anonymous throughout the course, as allowing them to be contacted may adversely affect competition.
Tenders sent by post or submitted directly must be submitted in a sealed envelope.
Electronic submissions must be encrypted.
Different provisions
In Germany, the contracting authority may entrust insured suppliers of external services with the printing and distribution of documentation, and their employees must be bound to observe confidentiality.
In Germany, tenders received must be marked with the date of receipt and sent directly and immediately in their original state (sealed) to the official in charge of their safekeeping.
That worker shall have no interest in the procurement procedure.
Tenders received must be locked until the opening date of the envelopes.
In the Czech Republic, tenders received are marked with a serial number.
The contracting authority shall draw up a report setting out the details of the contest, including the number of the tender and information on the preparation and receipt of the tender.
There are no specific provisions on the safekeeping of tenders.
Main control findings
BRH
BRH checked the assignment of the administration building.
Submission of tender documents
The audited contracting authorities of the building administration sent out the tender documentation either directly or entrusted its printing and distribution to an external company.
Self-employed workers did not send any documentation.
Information to be provided to tenderers
For the inquiry and information part of the procurement documents, the contracting authority did not follow the separate planning rule from the procurement procedure.
Contrary to the relevant regulations, he often did not mention the names of his contact staff in the documentation, but rather the staff of the project management department.
Some tendering notices even included the names of firms or self-employed persons involved in the preparation of the tender documents.
In isolated cases, tenderers who made enquiries during the procurement procedure did not receive identical information.
Menu Labelling
For the contracting authorities, all tenders received were marked with a stamp and an acknowledgement of receipt.
However, in some cases in negotiated procedures, tenders were accepted by self-employed professionals, in breach of the one-building rule for the prevention of corruption.
Custody of the tenders
Once marked, not all tenders were passed on directly and without delay to the official assigned to keep them in custody locked the tenders in a steel cabinet.
For one contracting authority, tenders were temporarily placed in an open slot in the mailroom to which all employees had access.
Another contractor placed the box in the corridor without constant supervision, and a portion of the bids dropped into the box stayed there overnight.
The opening of the box was wide and unsecured, making it accessible not only to employees.
SAO
The SAO did not find any shortcomings in this area.
Summary
In both countries, rules are laid down to prevent contacts, in particular between interested companies and design offices cooperating in the procurement process, throughout the whole process.
Equal treatment is ensured by sending the same information to all tenderers.
Rules to prevent manipulation and corruption are appropriate for the receipt, safekeeping and confidentiality of tenders.
In Germany the relevant requirements are somewhat more elaborated than in the Czech Republic.
In Germany, the control records collected showed breaches of the rules in certain areas.
These irregularities can easily be corrected.
Opening Envelopes
Opening Envelopes
During the opening of the envelopes, tenders that have been closed are opened, their contents are read and published.
The period during which a tenderer is bound by his tender shall begin at the time of expiry of the time-limit for submitting the envelopes or opening them and shall end at a time-limit specified by the contracting authority.
European law requirements and prevention of corruption
European legislation does not contain any specific provisions on the opening of envelopes.
However, national laws and regulations contain extensive requirements to protect bids and competitors during this sensitive phase of the entire procurement procedure.
The principles of ‘multiple eyes’, transparency and trustworthiness of the process of opening envelopes are intended to prevent manipulation and modification of tenders already submitted.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Same or similar provisions
The opening of envelopes must be carried out by several persons appointed by the contracting authority.
Compliance with the "multiple eyes" principle is intended to prevent individuals from covertly manipulating offers.
Applicants may be present when the envelopes are opened.
When opening, all envelopes must be placed in front of the chairman of the commission and are opened one by one.
Tenders received late shall not be taken into account.
In order to ensure transparency in the opening of envelopes, this stage, including the conclusions (results), must be recorded in writing. The record must show, inter alia, the names of the tenderers and the prices offered.
The minutes must be read and signed by the group leader.
The minutes may also be signed by the candidates present.
Different provisions
In the Czech Republic, a committee is set up for opening envelopes.
The participation of independent experts is permitted.
The members of the Commission may also be responsible for the subsequent stages of the procedure, i.e. the evaluation of the tenders.
In Germany, the opening of envelopes takes place in the presence of the manager and the registrar.
The manager must be an employee of the contracting authority.
The manager and, in the case of civil engineering, the recorder may not be involved in the preparatory phase (preparation of tender documents), may not decide on the award of the contract and may not be a member in the subsequent processing (management) of the contract.
The German regulations require that the record of the opening of envelopes include not only the offer prices, but also all information on discounts and special (unusual) signs, such as damaged envelopes on receipt or late receipt of an offer.
In the Czech Republic, in addition to the quotation prices, the written record must also contain an indication of the language of the bid and the signature authority of the bidder.
German law requires all parts of opened envelopes to be marked in order to prevent subsequent changes.
Tenders, usually marked by perforation with a date, must be kept confidential (secret) after the opening of the envelopes and may be made available only to persons who process them.
Similar measures do not exist in the Czech Republic.
If a single tender is received in the Czech Republic, the envelope may not be opened and the tenderer must be informed immediately.
In Germany, they do not have this arrangement, and if a single offer is accepted, it is opened and evaluated.
Main control findings
BRH
Cross-checking principle
During the opening of the envelopes, the contracting authorities adhered to the four-eyes principle.
There was always a manager and a recorder who was not assigned a different role.
In rare cases, however, for small negotiated procedures, natural persons have opened tenders sent to them without the participation of the contracting authority.
Envelope Opening Progress
The notes from the opening of the envelopes did not always document the process.
In some cases, tenders submitted late were not marked and in some cases signatures were missing.
Marking incoming menus
The contracting authorities marked the tenders with a date perforation, which was in accordance with the requirements.
Some of them had wrongly marked bids in the past, but changed their practice after the BRH had been alerted.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
SAO
Deadline for receipt of tenders
For road repairs, for one action, the tenderer's bid was submitted to the contracting authority with a delay of one month and was nevertheless accepted by the contracting authority.
Summary
The essential elements of the provisions for opening envelopes are identical in both countries, taking into account the principle of ‘more eyes’ and the principle of transparency, and thus make it possible to substantially reduce the risk of corruption and manipulation.
The German rules are more complex compared to the Czech Republic, especially the requirements for precise documentation of the procedure.
Marking, safe storage and respect for the confidentiality of tenders substantially limit the possibility of subsequent amendments to the tenders submitted.
In addition, the staff responsible for opening the envelopes may not carry out any other activities related to the contract to be awarded.
The findings, despite the discovery of some irregularities, confirm that the rules for opening envelopes are largely applied.
Assessment and evaluation of tenders
The contracting authority must examine and evaluate the tenders received within the time limit set.
At the same time, it must award the contract to the tenderer which best meets the award criteria in the tender.
European law requirements and prevention of corruption
EU law provides for a process of evaluation and evaluation of tenders in several steps:
The first step is to check candidates' suitability.
They must be in a good economic and financial position and possess the required professional and technical knowledge and skills.
Candidates who have ever been convicted of bribery or defrauding by a final judgment must always be excluded from procurement procedures.
The tenders of non-eliminated tenderers must be evaluated in terms of numerical accuracy, technical appropriateness and value for money on the basis of previously published award criteria.
The contracting authority must identify the most economically advantageous tender and, in particular, examine all appreciably low price tenders.
Review and evaluation is particularly sensitive and susceptible to manipulation.
For example, tenderers may be eliminated without a legitimate reason, or tenders that do not meet the requirements may be kept in competition.
The outcome of the evaluation may be wrongly influenced by the preference or disadvantage of a tenderer, thereby preventing the selection of the most economically advantageous tender.
The contracting authority must therefore take special measures in selecting the persons involved in the process and must ensure that they meet the requirements of good repute and technical knowledge.
They also need to make sure that the process and the decisions taken are transparent and credible.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Same or similar provisions
In line with EU requirements, the relevant provisions in both countries require a step-by-step examination and evaluation of tenders.
During the evaluation, the requirements to prove the suitability of the tenderers, the evaluation criteria and their weightings set out in the notice or the tender dossier may not be changed.
The criteria laid down for assessing the suitability of candidates may no longer be applied.
Further evaluation may only be carried out on the basis of evaluation criteria.
The tenders submitted must first be examined from a formal point of view as to whether they are complete or do not include suspicious elements that might encourage manipulation.
If tenderers are excluded from further examination and evaluation of their tenders, they must be informed immediately.
Where the evaluation of the tenders reveals a disproportionately low tender price, the tenderer concerned must be given the opportunity to comment on the situation before any elimination of its tender.
The reasons for the decision shall be transparently documented.
Different provisions
In Germany, tenderers must maintain confidentiality and only authorised persons may take part in the evaluation of tenders.
The mathematical accuracy of tenders may be reviewed only by the contracting authority.
Evaluation may only be entrusted to staff members who are not involved in the decision-making phase of the selection of the contractor or in the implementation phase of the project.
There are no precise provisions on who can carry out further reviews and evaluations.
When external companies are entrusted with the examination and evaluation of bids, they must be aware that bids are confidential and respect that fact honestly.
They may only make proposals for decisions to the contracting authority.
In the Czech Republic, tenders must be examined and evaluated by a committee appointed by the contracting authority, which draws up a report proposing the conclusion of the contract.
However, in both countries the final decision depends on the contracting authority.
If, in Germany, the documents and declarations required in connection with the submission of tenders are incomplete, the tenderers concerned must subsequently submit the required documents within six days.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
If tenderers do not comply with this obligation, their tenders must be excluded.
In the Czech Republic, the contracting authority may decide whether to give the tenderer the opportunity to complete the missing documents.
Failure to substantiate them will result in exclusion.
In Germany, it is necessary to examine not only the total price but also the unit prices.
Where the price structure of the tender indicates that prices for parts of the overall project have been estimated to be disproportionately high or low, tenderers must be asked for explanations.
If they do not provide an explanation and if the contracting authority can prove that the tender contains countervailing prices, such tenders must be excluded.
This is to prevent bidders from submitting price calculations on the assumption that substantial quantitative changes will be made to the different parts of the overall project, which may lead to a significant increase in project costs.
In the Czech Republic, the total price of a work is evaluated and the prices of individual parts are not examined in detail.
Valuation compensation is not a reason to exclude a bid.
In Germany, a contract cannot be awarded to a tenderer who offers a disproportionately high or low price.
Where one or more tenders differ in price by 10 % or more from the price estimated by the contracting authority or from other tenders, tenderers must prove that their tender has been credibly and convincingly calculated.
If they are unable to do so, the sponsor should exclude them.
In the Czech Republic, a tendering procedure may be cancelled after a price review if only tenders with disproportionately low prices have been received and the tenderers have not substantiated those prices.
If the price of the tender is significantly higher than the contracting authority’s estimate, this does not constitute a ground for refusal to award the contract to the tenderer concerned (unlike German procurement law).
If there is a suspicion of anti-competitive behaviour in Germany, the contracting authority is obliged to report this immediately to the competent authority.
The latter must decide what action to take and whether it is appropriate to inform the antitrust authority or law enforcement authorities.
In addition, they must approve (e.g. if the contract exceeds €50,000 and the contract has not been awarded to the lowest bidder) whether the contract will be awarded at contract negotiations or whether the procurement procedure will be cancelled.
Czech rules do not regulate this area.
Main control findings
BRH
Assessment of suitability and award criteria
Most of the procurement procedures examined were in line with the relevant requirements.
In the case of certain recommendations made by self-employed potential suppliers, the contracting authorities did not themselves check the suitability of the tenderers.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
In some cases of procurement procedures, insufficient documentation was provided as to whether, in making the choice, the contracting authority took into account all published evaluation criteria or whether the selection decision was based solely on the price criterion.
Selection of interested parties and confidentiality of tenders
Some contracting authorities used the services of non-authorised persons, such as self-employed persons or employees of building authorities.
They were integrated into the planning or implementation process of construction projects, performing functions that were to be performed exclusively by the contracting authority.
The contracting authorities tried to justify this by their insufficient human resources in the sector.
In some cases, they used self-employed professionals to examine offers of services from persons who were not formally bound to preserve the confidentiality of offers and to carry out their contractual obligations with due diligence.
As regards contracting authorities, there was generally no evidence of whether tenders held by self-employed professionals were available only to designated persons.
Examination and mathematical control of tenders
Some contracting authorities passed open envelopes for further examination to self-employed professionals without first carrying out the prescribed examination of tenders and mathematical examination and without documenting the results of such examination.
In the event of any intent to manipulate, third parties could make changes to tenders which could not be identified by the contracting authority.
Examination of the documents revealed a number of cases where unusual elements in the tenders were not adequately identified or where data on prices and products were missing.
Performance audits
A sample check by one contracting authority revealed that in 75 % of procurement procedures, the most advantageous tenders deviated by more than 10 % from the estimated costs.
One in 20 tendering procedures was cancelled due to inadequate tender prices.
In many cases, there was a lack of evidence or sufficient documentation on the examination of tenders or on negotiations with tenderers for further clarification.
Detailed information was also missing in many cases, e.g. on unit prices of the most important invoiced items.
Changes in market prices or increases in material costs were often cited as reasons for deviations.
The contracting authorities argued that the identification and demonstration of balancing prices was difficult.
In the case of one call for tenders, made by an entity outside the circle of standard contracting entities, a price 66 % lower than the initial cost estimate was offered.
No data were recorded on closer verification of cost or identification of reasons for deviations.
In clarifying this issue, the contracting authority did not find any indication of price dumping on the part of the tenderer to whom the contract might be awarded.
It was therefore not clarified whether the initial cost estimate was excessive or whether the price offered was too low.
The contracting authority later classified the tenderer as a ‘low-cost company’ which was unable to meet either the quality requirements or the deadlines.
In another procurement procedure, the contracting authority awarded the contract to a tenderer whose price was 35 % higher than the initial cost estimate.
The contracting authority did not annul the procurement procedure, arguing that the tenderer in question could be presumed not to have based its pricing on unreasonably high costs.
Suspicion of irregularities
Two cases of irregularities were found in one contracting authority and reported to the firm responsible for technical supervision.
In one procurement procedure, two tenderers submitted tenders containing identical price calculations, which prompted the contracting authority to exclude both tenders.
The inspections did not reveal any significant shortcomings in the application of the other provisions.
SAO
Suitability check
For many building projects, such as university buildings, roads and railways, contracting authorities did not exclude tenderers from participating in the procurement procedure even though they did not meet the requirements of the contracting authority as specified in the procurement specifications, or failed to provide evidence of qualification to the extent required, or, conversely, one contracting authority could not substantiate or explain the reason for the exclusion of a candidate who did.
Rating
In the case of the construction of a waste water treatment plant, it is true that the various evaluation criteria of the tendering procedure for the construction contractor were established, but they were not respected by the contracting authority during the procedure.
For the railway infrastructure development action, the weightings of the evaluation criteria for the award of the contract were not determined at the start of the procedure but only at the beginning of the evaluation.
For the road repair project, the assessment of the criterion of ‘shortening the construction period’ was not carried out correctly.
For the bidders in second and third place, the evaluation incorrectly indicated a greater reduction in construction time than indicated in their bids.
Documentation of the reasons for the selection of the tenderer
For the three housing promotion actions, the contracting authority did not substantiate the criteria on which it assessed the tenders received and how it selected the most suitable one.
Correctness of the selection of suppliers of small-scale contracts connected with the construction of a road circuit with an expected price of up to 500 000 CZK.
CZK could not be verified as the suppliers were selected only on the basis of telephone consultations on the price and the date of performance, but no documents about these negotiations were made.
Summary
In both countries, the rules for the verification and evaluation of bids are in line with EU requirements.
However, they differ with regard to the specific management steps and the choice of the individuals involved in the procedure.
Unlike the Czech Republic, in Germany these steps of the procurement process are governed by stricter and more detailed requirements, especially as regards the application of the cross-principle and the protection of tenders.
The same applies to the rules for verifying the appropriateness of the prices offered.
In both countries, it was found in some cases that the substantive steps of the process in the verification and evaluation of tenders were not documented at all and, if so, only partially.
The absence of control tools and transparency in decision-making during the examination and evaluation of tenders allows for manipulation.
The inspections showed serious deficiencies, such as the transmission of tenders to third parties without prior due diligence and the documentation of tenders by the contracting authority.
Where contracting entities entrust a third party with the performance of functions related to the screening and evaluation of tenders, appropriate measures and increased control of measures to prevent increased possibility of manipulation are necessary.
When checking the validity of prices, the audited German contracting authorities did not in all cases use the complete set of available instruments and did not deal with their own price structure.
In the Czech Republic, shortcomings were found in the evaluation of the suitability of tenders and the use of evaluation criteria.
Completion of procurement procedure
After examination and evaluation of the tenders, the procurement procedure is concluded by the award of the contract or the cancellation of the procedure.
European law requirements and prevention of corruption
In the case of a European procedure, all tenderers must be informed in advance of the award decision.
Suppliers shall have the opportunity to review the decision taken within a limited period of 10 or 15 days and to request a review if appropriate.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
The deadline of 10 days applies to electronic submissions and 15 days to other submissions.
The contract may not be concluded before the expiry of that period or before the decision of the review procedure.
Where the review body rejects the complaint and upholds the decision, candidates and tenderers must be given the opportunity to have the correctness of the application of procurement law reviewed by an independent court.
This measure ensures that no contracting authority will be able to circumvent the legal protection of suppliers by swiftly implementing a procurement decision.
The contracting authority shall document the course of the procedure appropriately.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Same or similar provisions
Both countries implemented management on information obligations.
In both countries, the final decision on the outcome of the procurement procedure rests with the contracting authority.
However, the latter cannot arbitrarily depart from the results of a properly carried out examination and evaluation.
The contracting authority shall conclude the contract within a specified period of time.
This is because, after that period, the tenderer is not bound by its tender.
The content of the contract must not deviate from the winning bid.
Unsuccessful tenderers are to be informed of the result in good time, indicating the reason why their bid was not accepted.
Cancellation of a procurement procedure is permissible only if certain conditions are met, e.g. if the prices in the tenders do not correspond to the specifications.
Different provisions
In the Czech Republic, the mandatory waiting period before the conclusion of a contract of 10 or 15 days applies to both European and national procedures.
In Germany, an equivalent provision applies only to European procedures.
In all other procedures in Germany, tenderers cannot be informed of the award of a contract before a decision on its EU provisions and a review award has been taken.
In the Czech Republic, a procurement procedure must be cancelled if a single tender has been received or if only one tender meets the requirements for its consideration and evaluation.
In Germany, a contract must be awarded even if a single tender has been received, provided that the tender fulfils the conditions set out in the procurement documents and is cost-effective.
Main control findings
BRH
The audits found few irregularities in the completion of the audited procurement procedures.
In some European proceedings, companies have received letters of authorisation before the expiry of the notification period, which may invalidate the contract.
Also, in several open procedures, empowerment letters contained modifications incompatible with the content of the tender, such as new deadlines.
In some cases, contracting authorities did not meet the deadlines for awarding the contract.
In one open procedure, the contracting authority selected the second most advantageous tender because a tenderer offering a price more than 50 % lower no longer wished to perform the contract at the price initially offered.
SAO
In one case, during the reconstruction and construction of a university, the contracting authority did not conclude a contract for work with the contractor in accordance with the draft contract contained in the tender of the selected tenderer.
For some road repair and maintenance actions, the construction of a motorway and a ring road, contracts were concluded later than provided for by law.
For building modifications in former military sites, the contracting authority sent a notice of selection of the most suitable tender to all tenderers after the statutory deadline had expired.
Summary
Both countries have implemented EU rules on the closure of procurement procedures.
Legal protection of unsuccessful candidates contributes to reducing corruption.
The Czech Public Procurement Act includes the legal protection institute even in cases of contracts below the EU thresholds.
In both countries it was found that the limited time limit for the conclusion of the contract was exceeded.
This may cause adverse economic consequences and increase the risk of manipulation.
In the future, contracting entities should eliminate such shortcomings in the conclusion of procurement procedures.
Documentation, notifications, information obligations
The procurement process, with all its different stages, relevant findings and decisions, including the relevant justification, shall be documented in a transparent manner.
The completion of the procurement procedure at European level must be published.
The data on procurement procedures are summarised in EU statistics.
European law requirements and prevention of corruption
The obligation to document procurement procedures is in line with the fundamental EU-wide requirement of transparency.
Only proper and time-relevant decision-making and reasoning documentation guarantees transparency in the procurement process and, in particular, guarantees effective legal protection of the interests of each tenderer in cases where the value of the contract exceeds European thresholds.
Appropriate documentation is also an essential part of anti-corruption measures, as the obligation to provide detailed reasons for individual decisions and their merits significantly reduces the scope for manipulation.
In the case of European procurement procedures, the contracting authority must notify the completion of the procedures to the EU’s Office of Official Publications within 48 days from the date of award of the contract.
The Office publishes the notification in the Official Journal of the EU and this also fulfils the transparency requirement.
The contracting authority must summarise the essential details of its procurement procedures and communicate them to the European Commission through the competent national ministries.
On this basis, the European Commission publishes annual statistics to provide an overview of the development of the single market in the construction sector.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Same or similar provisions
Both countries have fully transposed into their national legislation the provisions of the European law on documentation and notification obligations.
The provisions on documentation and notification for European statistics are almost identical in both countries.
Different provisions
In the Czech Republic, the obligation to notify the award does not only apply to European procedures, but also to national procurement procedures.
The notification period is only 15 days from the conclusion of the contract.
In Germany, the obligation to notify procurement procedures, in addition to those that are mandatory under European law, applies only to restricted procedures exceeding the limit of €25,000 ($625,000) and direct procedures exceeding the limit of €15,000 ($375,000) including VAT.
Below this limit, only interested parties shall be informed.
Main control findings
BRH
Documentation
A large number of assignments have not been fully documented.
The notification did not contain all the necessary information, such as the reasons for the delivery specifications and national rules.
In one procedure, the contracting authority did not explain why it had concluded a contract with a tenderer whose tender did not contain the mandatory elements.
Some of the required records were also missing in several cases.
Publication of contract award
In the case of some European procedures, the contracting authority erred in publishing the notice.
In the case of several awards, there was no evidence of publication of the notice.
Notification obligation
The statistically verified files included the required minimum of information and were created and sent on time.
Preliminary transparency
In most cases, the contracting authority published information about the outcome of the contract.
In the case of one contracting authority, the contract information was not published due to a communication error.
It took the contracting authority six months to notice the error.
Consequently, the outcome of the procedure was not made public during that period.
SAO
Documentation
For events related to the organisation of a major sporting event, significant shortcomings were found in the documentation of the procurement procedure, such as incorrect quotation prices in the evaluation committee minutes.
In addition, some documents about opening envelopes were not signed.
Publication of contract award
In preparation for the repair and maintenance of roads, the construction of a motorway and a ring road, the contract notices were sent out after the deadline or were not published.
Summary
The provisions on documentation and notification and notification obligations cover European requirements and are almost identical in both countries.
This generally ensures the transparency of procurement procedures and final selection.
The Czech reporting obligations are somewhat stricter compared to the German ones.
The inspections revealed deficiencies substantially diminishing the value of documentation as a means of preventing corruption.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
Contracting entities should continue to enhance the transparency of their procurement procedures and fully comply with the notification and notification requirements.
Summary evaluation and recommendations
Parallel audits of the SAO and BRH focused on two areas that are essential for the public procurement process in both the Czech Republic and Germany, namely the application of EU procurement law and the prevention of corruption.
EU legislation provides an opportunity for international competition within the EU to create a single European market.
Measures to prevent corruption are not only aimed at avoiding or at reducing corruption, but also at preventing financial damage and possible loss of confidence in the integrity of the government.
The audit findings of the two audit institutions – SAO and BRH – with regard to the application of the procurement law of both countries and the comparison of its relevant provisions give an internal view of common and different elements that can help both countries in further development and application of this law.
Application of EU procurement law
Both countries have transposed EU public procurement law into their national legislation.
There is thus an important common basis for both countries to award public contracts and meet basic competition requirements. equal treatment of candidates; transparency of procurement procedures.
Contracting entities reinforce these requirements in part by very far-reaching measures.
The Czech Republic has extended the provisions on public procurement requiring a European procedure to procedures that are below the EU thresholds and are therefore governed by national legislation.
However, the inspections showed that contracting entities are not always fully compliant with all relevant provisions of EU requirements.
German contracting authorities should continue to improve the quality of their procurement procedures in order to eliminate these irregularities, taking into account the implementation of EU requirements.
In the Czech Republic, the audits identified risks affecting the economy and the achievement of value for money.
These include, in particular, the incomplete definition of the subject-matter of the contract, which resulted in additional works that were often unlawfully awarded, an insufficient definition of the estimated price, and the requisite qualifications, which narrowed the potential pool of tenderers and thus reduced competition in the field of transport construction in particular.
Preventing corruption
The joint inspections were not intended to detect cases of corruption.
The reason was that both BRH and SAO did not have sufficient tools for this.
However, they can check whether the competent authorities of both countries are taking measures to reduce corruption and manipulation in public procurement and how these measures are being applied.
As regards the prevention of corruption, the two countries focused on different aspects.
In particular, the German provisions allow for broad and unrestricted competition, transparency and controls as a central element in the prevention of corruption.
In particular, the Czech regulations aim at enhancing the transparency of procurement procedures.
BRH
In Germany, there are general principles of corruption prevention that apply to the entire federal administration.
These principles have also been incorporated into public procurement requirements and have been adopted to meet the specific needs of such procurement.
The relevant provisions specify, sometimes with great precision, the means of complying with the requirements to prevent corruption during the procurement procedure and to select the persons involved.
In the opinion of BRH, the principle of preference for open procurement procedures and calls for participation is a particularly appropriate instrument to substantially reduce the risk of corruption in public procurement, while restricting competition increases the risk of manipulation.
Risk analysis and adequate controls (in particular the cross-checking principle), transparency requirements, tender documentation and various organisational measures are also effective mechanisms to prevent corruption and manipulation.
The BRH therefore does not feel the need for substantial changes to the current provisions that public institutions have to comply with when awarding public contracts.
Anti-corruption measures are widely applied in practice.
Irregularities found in some areas highlighted weaknesses in compliance with certain requirements.
Therefore, contracting entities should provide their staff with further training to familiarise them with anti-corruption requirements and further raise their awareness in this area.
A significant part of the weaknesses identified relate to activities delegated by the building administration to private entrepreneurs.
However, the lack of adequate controls on the delegation of powers increases the risk of abuse.
Activities susceptible to manipulation and corruption should therefore only be transferred to third parties if the administration can guarantee a sufficient degree of protection against unauthorised interference.
SAO
In the Czech Republic, anti-corruption strategies have been adopted at government level, which also significantly affect the area of public procurement.
The SAO audits took place during the period when one of the most important parts of this strategy was implemented, namely the significant amendment of the Public Procurement Act and the adoption of subsequent implementing regulations.
The adopted strategy and new legislation can be effective steps to improve the situation.
Under the new rules, for example, the contracting authority must justify the effectiveness of the contract, the determination of the estimated value of the contract, the adequacy of the qualification requirements, the establishment of the basic evaluation criteria and the method of evaluation of the tenders, and document and publish everything properly.
Recommendations
Overall, the SAO and the Czech National Bank (SAO) have a favourable opinion on ways of implementing EU requirements.
Their wide application in practice has confirmed that EU competition, equal treatment and transparency requirements form an appropriate basis for public procurement within the EU.
The transnational model in place in the EU can also serve as a basis for similar projects aimed at facilitating and promoting international competition.
Bearing in mind the great potential of material and non-material damage resulting from manipulation and corruption in connection with public procurement, both BRH and the SAO insist that it is the responsibility of contracting authorities to continue and improve measures against corruption and manipulation where possible.
In particular, organisational structures and procurement processes should be continuously monitored for weaknesses and risks in order to identify and respond appropriately to the need for corrective action.
Compared to the practice in Germany, it has emerged that the share of open procurement procedures in the Czech Republic, which give room to competition and substantially reduce the possibility of corrupt behaviour, should increase.
The award of contracts in lots may also have a positive impact on the price achieved, where this is permissible and appropriate for the contracting authorities. A higher number of bidders puts pressure on the price achieved.
The organisation of the procurement procedure also has an influence on the conduct of the procurement procedure.
In Germany, it is organised by special state organisations, while in the Czech Republic, with the exception of transport constructions, it is organised by the central authorities themselves or through contractual organisations.
The SAO and BRH wish to further develop their findings in their work, where appropriate, and to address them with their parliament and government beyond their joint project.
Moreover, they are convinced that the principles of procurement law and their implementation, as described in this report, will also be of interest at the level of the International Organisation of Supreme Audit Institutions (INTOSAI), as these principles and findings are of general importance for the effective fight against corruption and for guaranteeing that public contracts are awarded under a regime of well-functioning and fair competition and that value for money is achieved.
